♠ Original name ♣ Parthian Empire I ♥ "The Parthians were originally a nomadic tribe, the Parni, settled in Parthia, and gradually they came to be called by the name of the territory."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_1_IrPart1]
♠ Alternative names ♣ Arsacid Empire; Parthia; Parthians; Arsacid Kingdom ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_2_IrPart1] Arsacid kingdom.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_3_IrPart1] Arsacid empire.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_4_IrPart1]
♠ Peak Date ♣ 120 BCE ♥"The first Parthian ruler, Arsaces, established the dynasty approximately 240 b.c.e. ... The real founder of the Parthian empire was Mithridates I, who ascended the throne in 171. He conquered western Iran, reaching Media in 155 and Seleucia in 141. ... the Parthians definitely established their hold on Babylonia by the time of Mithridates II, ca. 120 b.c.e. and held it until ca. 226 c.e., with brief intervals of Roman occupation."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_5_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Period when the empire expanded under Mithridates I. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_6_IrPart1] "The geographical expansion of Parthia and the political consolidation of the early Parthian kingdom is associated with Mithradates (Mihrdad) I (c. 171-138 BCE)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_7_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Few rulers in Arsacid history were as distinguished as Mithradates I. During his reign, the Parthian state was transformed from an insignificant political center to a vast and mighty empire. The transformation was the fruit of his large-scale policy of expansion."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_8_IrPart1] also known as Mithradates I the Great who reigned c171-132 BCE.
♠ Duration ♣ 247 BCE - 40 CE ♥In the absence of surviving written documents, chronology of Parthian rulers is based on coinage.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_9_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Start: 247 BCE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Arsaces, of Scythian or Bactrian origin, was elected leader of the Parni tribes in 247 BCE. This date marks the beginning of the Arsacid era."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_10_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Less than ten years after the rebellion of Andragoras against the Seleucids, in 238 BCE, Arsaces and his brother Tiridates invaded the satrapy of Parthia, killed Andragoras and established control over this province."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_11_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] It is believed to be the start of the Parthian's revolt against the Seleucids, as well as the coronation of the second Parthina king, Tiridates I. As such it can been seen as the end of Selucid authority in the province.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_12_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]End 226 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Parthians fought three battles against the Sassanians and lost each one. Reign of the last Parthian king Artabanus IV ends; Sasanian rule begins. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_13_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Although Ardashir took the Parthian capital, Ctesiphon, and probably with it the title 'King of Kings', in 226 CE the Parthian king Vologases VI minted coins in his hame at least to 228 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_14_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]
Periodization c240-172 BCE; 171 BCE - 40 CE; 40 - 226 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]240-172 BCE (pre-Imperial, Seleucid Empire period)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The first Parthian ruler, Arsaces, established the dynasty approximately 240 b.c.e. ..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_15_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]
171 BCE - 40 CE (Empire period)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "... The real founder of the Parthian empire was Mithridates I, who ascended the throne in 171. He conquered western Iran, reaching Media in 155 and Seleucia in 141. ... the Parthians definitely established their hold on Babylonia by the time of Mithridates II, ca. 120 b.c.e. and held it until ca. 226 c.e., with brief intervals of Roman occupation."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_16_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Mithradates I ... one of the first powerful Parthian monarchs, attacked Demetrius, the Seleucid ruler ... Mithradates conquered Susa and its hinterlands shortly before 140 B.C. and installed a Parthian administration that probably survived for most of the next century. We know that by A.D. 21 Susa was under Parthian control, for in a letter of this date, written in Greek, Artabanus III, the Parthian sovereign, validated a contested election at Susa."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_17_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 1st century BCE: "from then the first open conflicts occurred between Parthian rulers and aristocrats."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_18_IrPart1] 53 BCE was the Parthian victory at the Battle of Carrhae - this would have increased Roman interest in supporting rivals to Parthian throne, and in fact the battle of Carrhae may have been initiated by the Romans to take advantage of Parthian disorder in Babylonia.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] c92 BCE Mithradates II was challenged by a usurper "who had probably gained control over the empire's eastern satrapies, supported by local rich and influential aristocratic families, and over a large part of Mesopotamia. However, civil war was prevented by the king's natural death."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_19_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Babylonia "was not touched by the Romans in their invasion of 54-3, or by Antony in 39-31 during his unsuccessful Armenian adventure."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_20_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Between c-100 BCE to 100 CE the Babylonians at Babylon ceased to exist while the Greeks at Seleucia ceased to have the importance they once did.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
40 - 226 CE (less centralized, fuedal period)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "the Parthian state was highly unstable, and Artabanus' death at about A.D. 40, in combination with financial and military reverses over the preceding decades, apparently weakened the Parthian state to the extent that it no longer issued an imperial coinage and successful revolts were staged at Seleucia-on-the-Tigris and other cities. At about this same time, it appears that Susa and its environs were incorporated into the 'satrapy' of Elymais (Fig. 6)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_21_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Written documents (mainly in Greek, Latin, or Hebrew) from the first two centuries A.D. in Southwest Asia suggest that the Parthian 'Empire' was at most times an unstable coalition of vassal states brought periodically under imperial Parthian control."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_22_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Elymais coined its own money, conducted its own public works programs, and in other was was apparently independent until about A.D. 215, when, documentary evidence suggests, the Parthian imperial government was once again in control at Susa."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_23_IrPart1] Elymais/Susiana region experienced an upturn in economy, agriculture, population[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The last hundred years in the life of Parthia was a period appropriately described as the 'downfall of the Parthian Empire'. The period began with the reign of Vologases II, who ruled until A.D. 146/7." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_24_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "By the beginning of the third century A.D., the states of southern Mesopotamia and the provinces of eastern Iran - Margiana, Segistan (Sistan) and Kerman - were virtually independent states, governed by local dynasties which only formally recognized their dependence on the Arsacids."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_25_IrPart1] nominal centralization
♠ Degree of centralization ♣ confederated state ♥ The Parthian Empire was divided into three areas: self governing kingdoms in Mesopotamia; semi-independent kingdoms on the borders of Iran); and Parthian 'heartlands' in the centre under rule of the King.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_26_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR][MAJIDBENAM_REF_27_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Written documents (mainly in Greek, Latin, or Hebrew) from the first two centuries A.D. in Southwest Asia suggest that the Parthian 'Empire' was at most times an unstable coalition of vassal states brought periodically under imperial Parthian control."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_28_IrPart1]
♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ alliance ♥"There was, indeed, a separate Parthian kingdom beyond the Indus, independent of the Arsacid kingdom, but in friendly alliance with it." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_29_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Alliance with China against the Kushans during the reign of Vologases.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_30_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]There was diplomatic contact with the Chinese. A Parthian general met a Chinese envoy in c.199 BCE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_31_IrPart1]
♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ Confederacy of the Dahae ♥ "Arsaces, of Scythian or Bactrian origin, was elected leader of the Parni tribes in 247 BCE. This date marks the beginning of the Arsacid era. The Parni or Aparni were part of the confederacy of the Dahae and lived along the river Ochus southeast of the Caspian Sea."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_32_IrPart1] Originally the Parthian were a nomadic tribe, called the Parni, from northeastern Iran. The date of their invasion of ‘Parthia’ is usually given as 247 BCE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_33_IrPart1]
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ elite migration ♥ "The language of Parthia at this time was Parthian, which linguistically is related to Median and belongs to the family of West Iranian languages. The Parni newcomers abandoned their own language in favour of Parthian. The Parni, whose speech was described by Justin as 'midway between Scythian and Median, and contained features of both', now became known as the Arsacids or Parthians."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_34_IrPart1]
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ Parthian Empire II ♥
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ Greco-Bactrian ♥ "The Chinese records revealed that the Parthians shared many cultural characteristics with the petty kingdoms in the Sogdiana, Bactria, and Ferghana regions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_35_IrPart1] The records "suggests that the oases between the Pamirs and the Amu-darya were occupied by people who were culturally related to each other, probably all of Iranian stock." "For example, the Tochari kingdom in the Amu-darya was closely associated with the Parthians in terms of products, customs, trade and currency; the Samarkand was similar to the Tochari, the Bactria to the Sogdiana, the Ferghana to both the Tochari and the Parthians."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_36_IrPart1]

♠ Capital ♣ Nisa; Hekatompylos; Rhagae; Ectatana; Ctesiphon ♥ Asaak (Astauene) established by Arsaces I. New Nisa up to first century BCE. "At later stages, the functions of a capital city were also served by Hecatompylos, Ecbatana, Rhagae, Babylon, and Ctesiphon."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_37_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]near Ashkhabad. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_38_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Parthian capital referred to as Hekatompylos in Chinese records. "The first capital of the Parthians was Nisa in the province of Parthia. In about 217 BCE, the Parthian capital was moved to Hekatompylos, which remained as the main capital of the Parthian empire till c. 50 BCE ... During that period, Rhagae (Rayy), Ecbatana, and Ctesiphon near the river Tigris were also selected as capitals."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_39_IrPart1]
♠ Language ♣ Greek; Pahlavi ♥ "After the introduction of Old Persian cuneiform under the Achaemenians, a related form of the same language (a Middle Persian language written in a simplified form of the Aramaic consonantal alphabet) continued as the vehicle of administration under the Parthians."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_40_IrPart1] Parthian. Aramaic and Greek also in use. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_41_IrPart1] Pahlavi was the official lan­guage of the Parthians, which is "Persian written in Aramaic characters."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_42_IrPart1] "Greek served as their official language."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_43_IrPart1] "Although we know little of Parthian administrative practice we may assume it provided the basis for the Sasanian administration that followed it in a closely related form of Middle Persian, and in a related script, in the 3rd century AD."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_44_IrPart1]♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European ♥ "The language of Parthia at this time was Parthian, which linguistically is related to Median and belongs to the family of West Iranian languages. ♠ Alternate Religion ♣ ♥Map of Parthian Empire c. 60 BCE [source: wiki commons][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Parthian Empire in 1 CE [source: wiki commons][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The first ruler of the Parthian dynasty was Arsaces who lived at about 240 BCE[MAJIDBENAM_REF_47_IrPart1] but the founder of the Parthian Empire (171 BCE - 40 CE period) was Mithridates I, who initiated the Parthian conquest of Persia and Babylonia from the Seleucids.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_48_IrPart1] The Parthians were essentially "a military aristocracy"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_49_IrPart1] that ruled the many different peoples (e.g. Persians, Greeks, Jews, Babylonians) who lived in Persia and Babylonia.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Parchments and ostra found at Dura-Europas show the Parthians had "an extensive and developed bureaucracy".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_50_IrPart1] The Parthians, who were originally a nomadic tribe (the Parni) from northeastern Iran[MAJIDBENAM_REF_51_IrPart1], conquered Seleucid territory that already possessed municipal, provincial (satrapal) and central government. The Parthians had some centralized institutions but these were not based at a capital city but "moved from city to city along with their administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_52_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Parthian nobility was inserted into the regional governance structures they inherited[MAJIDBENAM_REF_53_IrPart1] as satraps appointed by the king, while much of the rest of the territory consisted of directly granted personal fiefs[MAJIDBENAM_REF_54_IrPart1] or vassal kingdoms.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_55_IrPart1] In terms of central government the Parthian Arsacids retained the Achaemenid model (as had the Seleucids) which had departments called diwans "responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation." The departments were run by individuals called dibirs who were themselves responsible to a first-minister.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_56_IrPart1]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ 280,000: 200 BCE; 2,000,000: 100 BCE; 3,000,000: 1 CE ♥ squared kilometers.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]280,000: 200 BCE; 2,000,000: 100 BCE; 3,000,000: 1 CE; 1,900,000: 100 CE; 1,800,000: 200 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Maximum extent should include Oman region?[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "the Gulf region shared to a great extent in a common cultural kione with Babylonia, Mesene, Susiana, Elymais and Karmania between the second century BC and the third century AD. The Periplus leaves little doubt that some measure of Parthian authority extended to the Oman peninsular during the first century AD ... other late sources certainly suggests that Ardashir encountered a Parthian ruler in eastern Arabia when he campaigned there near the end of his reign. The Parthian presence in the Gulf, less widely acknowledged perhaps than either the earlier Seleucid or the later Sasanian one, was nevertheless a reality."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_60_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Coinage of Vologases IV (r.147-191 CE) found in Eastern Arabia (e.g. Jebel Kenzan).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_61_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The campaigns of Mithridates I (171-138 BCE) spread the authority of the Parthian kings. In 113 BCE Mithridates II took the title of ‘King of Kings’. For 300 years from 92 BC, the Parthian Empire was seen as the main foe of the Roman Empire. Parthian power derived from their military successes and control of commerce. Trade flourished as Parthia was an intermediary between Rome and Far East and became part of the network of Silk Roads.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_62_IrPart1]

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ [4-5] ♥1. Capital (Nisa; Hekatompylos; Rhagae; Ectatana; Ctesiphon)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]2. Regional capitals[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
3. Towns
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
4. Villages
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
(5. Hamlets?)
♠ Administrative levels ♣ [5-6] ♥The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities, each appropriate to the area governed, to whom they maintained a feudal relationship and to whose subjects they desired little direct relationship at all."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_73_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The provinces and cities outside the Parthia heartlands paid tribute or allegiance to the ‘king of kings’, but regional lords retained their own power. Although the Parthians themselves were Zoroastrian, the empire was multi-ethnic and multi-religious and revolts against the Parthian King were common. There were also challenges form within the Parthian elites. Parthian Kings were chosen from the Arascid clan, but they were 'appointed' by nobles rather than automatically succeeding to rule. After 40 BCE Parthian military power was weakening and they could not mount offensive operations into Roman territory. They suffered a series of military defeats to the Romans and a smallpox epidemic between 161 CE and 217 CE. However it was invasion by the Sassanians from Iran that ended their rule.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_74_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]
1. King[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Parthian King of Kings[MAJIDBENAM_REF_75_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Council of Nobles[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Council of Magi
These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_76_IrPart1]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]_Court_[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Parthian kings presided over a considerable body of specialized officials and functionaries of various levels dealing with the collection of duties and taxes and supervising local communities and administrative units. It should be noted that while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_77_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan). The king was traditionally elected by the nobles from the members of the Arsacid family, although the succession of the eldest son was not guaranteed. Below the king were the members of the six noble families, probably modeled after the six noble families of the Achaemenid court and continuing this tradition into the Sasanian period. These families all seem to have had a control of a section of the country where the majority of their land possessions were located. They also held particular positions in the royal court, members of each family having the privilege of crowning the king, serving in his bed-chamber, or serving as the first minister."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_78_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"We know that varied schools of philosophy flourished in Hellenistic Babylonia, and Greek metaphysicians, astronomers, naturalists, historians, geographers, and physicians worked there. The Parthian court made considerable use of such trained and able men for building its bureaucracy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_79_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. First Minister
"The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_80_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Unlike the Seleucid and Sasanian periods, the Arsacid empire is very poorly documented in terms of its administration." So discussion of the relationship between center and periphery in the Empire and how its main institutions were run is speculative to some extent. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_81_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Dibir of a Diwan
"The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwāns, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_82_IrPart1]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Official dealing with revenue[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "there was a general state cadastre for the lands of the royal domain. The state fixed and strictly controlled tax revenue."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_83_IrPart1]
4.
"Records found in excavations at Nisa provide evidence of different types of tax collection, depending on the category of the land. Two categories are known - patbaz and uzbari. Patbaz was collection in kind for the use of the king. It is less clear what the other category was. There are also indications of the existence of special levies for the support of religious activities, somewhat similar to tithes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_84_IrPart1]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]_Provincial government_[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"as the Parthians conquered Seleucid territory, they found in addition to the various municipal governments a decayed satrapal system, built upon the eparchs and hyparchs. The Parthian nobility had, in places, to be superimposed upon this structure. Various relatives and followers were granted 'fuedal' fiefs."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_85_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Part of the kingdom was divided into satrapies ruled by satraps appointed by the king. The rest consisted of vassal kingdoms."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_86_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Satrap of Satraps
Mithradates II rock reliefs at Behistun 87 BCE show "his principal officials ... The chief of these is called satrap of satraps, the other three simply satraps. Probably these men belonged to the great families of Iran such as the Surens and Karens."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_87_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Satrap
"Apart from the territories forming part of the royal domain and governed through satraps, much of Parthia consisted of vassal kingdoms."City officials (e.g. prefect)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_88_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "There was an extensive and developed bureaucracy, as attested by ostraca from Nisa and by the Parthian parchments and ostraca from Dura-Europos."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_89_IrPart1] This quote might be referring to the provincial administration:[MAJIDBENAM_BR] check parthiansources.com e.g. text SKZ[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4. Parthian town
"While sources also speak of 'Parthian towns', in contrast to Greek ones, there is no specific information about their internal life. It can only be conjectured that they did not enjoy autonomy and were under the full control of the local Parthian administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_90_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] City officials (e.g. prefect)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_91_IrPart1] -- does this reference refer to towns directly controlled or to the Greek city-states?[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 5. dïz (group of villages) headed by a dïzpat

[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Local Kingdoms [MAJIDBENAM_REF_101_IrPart1]
"From the reign of Mithradates I onwards, when the Parthians controlled Mesopotamia and Iran, local kingdoms were granted a certain amount of freedom, as long as they recognised the Parthian sovereign as their overlord."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_102_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] In Macedonian times "Alexander and Peucestas apparently did touch neither the Achaemenid system of local dependencies and local administration, nor the basic ideas of the Persian ideology of kingship. There is no other way to explain the fact that nothing is known about unrest in Persis after Peucestas' appointment, that the new satrap could levy troops there without difficulty, and that a great number of nobles collaborated with Alexander. This kind support from the nobles for their new persophile Macedonian masters continued until the second century BCE".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_103_IrPart1]
♠ Religious levels ♣ 3 ♥"many religions flourished within the Parthian empire; in addition to Judaism, various Hellenistic, Babylonian, and other cults were observed in the cities of Dura, Palmyra, and elsewhere. There can be no doubt, moreover, that the Good Religion of the Mazdayasnians continued to be cultivated. Though Zoroastrianism as we know it from Sasanid times may have taken shape in the Arsacid period, we have no way to trade its developent. In general, the Parthians were as flexible and tolerant in religious matters as they were in politics and, at best, in Frye's judgment, we may speak of a 'general Mazdayasnian religious predominace,' within which were many subdivisions and even aberrations. Nonetheless, the dominant influence in Parthian religion was that of the Magi, who were, as in later periods, in charge of formal rites and cultic acitivities, though their influence on the religions of the Babylonian area was limited by the existence of powerful competing traditions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_104_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]_Mazdayasnianism_[MAJIDBENAM_BR]

_Mithraism_[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Cult of Mithra spread from the Parthian Empire to Rome (originated in India? bronze age?). "Contrary to other religions of the same type, such as the cults of Isis and Osiris, Serapis, Dionysus (all well-known examples), Mithraicism eschewed any external manifestations and depended only on its initiatory nature to recruit its followers. ... it gradually became a common faith for soldiers, civil servants, merchants ... The members joined a spirituality of an initiatory type ... shared with a large group of solar faiths ... that promised both a life near to the deity and a personal redemption."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_107_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"From the end of the first century B.C.E. we have evidence of a cult coming from the East and gradually and discretely conquering the Roman army and administration (Daniels). This god, previously unknown to the Romans, was called Mithra. Some historians believe (see Plutarch, Pomp. 24.7) that the notorius Cilician pirates defeated by Pompeius propagated this cult when deported in Calabria. We now believe that it was a late transformation of the god Mithra, the friendly protector of contracts .... and defender of true and just causes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_108_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"We must also stress that this god retained, in his manifestation in the Roman Empire, his essential characteristics of friend and guardian of contracts."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_109_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In Rome "Mithra probably won over even the imperial house. We are wary about the well-known initiation of the emperor Nero to the mysteries of the Magi through Tiridates (see Turcan 1989:237). However, it seems that the emperor Commodus (192) was an unworthy adept of the mysteries, because he was suspected of having killed a fellow-adept during a ceremony simulating a ritual sacrifice. The imperial house had a much worthier adept in Diocletian and his colleagues of the Tetrarchy: Galerius and Licinius. The god is then called the fautor imperii sui, the "protector of the imperial power" (Inscription of Carnuntum in 307)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_110_IrPart1] Mithracism was very successful in the Parthian Empire.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Mithracism: "on the social level people learned, in the 'Persic Cavern', to respect the contract linking the human being to the cosmos and to the gods, and then, at least in an implicit way, to respect the emperors, who were divine beings, as intermediaries between the sky and the earth. The faithfulness to a vivifying cosmic order was thus accompanied by faithfulness to the one representing this order on earth. It is not surprising, then, that Mithra was invoked as Jupiter Dolichenus for the salvation of the emperor. In time, a cult ascribed to the enemies gets mixed up with the worship of the protecting gods of Rome!"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_111_IrPart1]
♠ Military levels ♣ 6 ♥"Parthia was the only state that rivaled Rome at the same level of sophisticated political and military organization". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_112_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]
1. King[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Commanders (aristocracy)
"The aristocracy provided the cataphracts and trained their retainers - not their slaves - as mounted bowmen. Parthian army was small, as in most feudal states, never more than 6,000 cataphracts and 34,000 bowmen..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_113_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Cataphract level senior officer inferred - leader of 1000?
4. Cataphract level junior officer inferred - leader of 100?
5. Leader of 10 horsemen inferred
At Carrhae, a Parthian general named Surenas brought with him "a train of camels, one for every ten horsemen, loaded with spare arrows."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_114_IrPart1] This reference to the supplying of groups of 10 horsemen is interesting because the Sassanids, the Parthian successors, inherited their military structure (division between heavy and light cavalry). It is noted of the Sassanids that they "Like the Achaemenids ... likely used the decimal system to organize the Spah (army). The title Hazarmard/Hazarbad means "chief of a thousand"." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_115_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 6. Individual soldier

♠ Professional military officers ♣ inferred absent ♥ The commanders would have been formed out of a warrior aristocracy who had inherited the position of leading forces and land which they used to support themselves and their troops. AD: coded inferred present as officers in the lower hierarchical ranks might have been more specialised (eg. leader of 10 horsemen).[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The Parthians were a warrior people. Though possessing armoured knights mounted on weight-carrying chargers."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_116_IrPart1] no regular army they were superb horsemen and and archers, and in time of war the nobility provided heavily a[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The division between grand and petty nobility was reflected in the structure of the Parthian army. Both provided the cavalry - indeed, during the first century BC the Persian infantry almost completely disappeared - but this was made up of two distinct types of horsemen. On the one hand, there were the mounted archers, lightly armed on nimble mounts, who were supplied by the lesser estate holders. Most of these were from Eastern Iran where they lived in small castles and block-houses, and evolved a typical feudal culture centered round jousting, hunting, war, and a chivalric code that emphasized the virtues of personal honour and the protection of women. The grandees, on the other hand, supplied a new type of horseman, a development of the Sarmatian and the Saka knight, who encased both himself and his horse in mail armour and armed himself with a great bow, lance and sword."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_117_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"We may suppose that the Arsacids thus preserved the original nomadic nobility, rewarded them for their services by gifts of land which would provide a base for future political and military power."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_118_IrPart1]

They may have been "specially trained professional warrior slaves". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_119_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Non-professional infantry[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The mass of lesser nobles and their retainers were traditional horse archers, mounted on tough steppe ponies and armed with the reflex bow."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_120_IrPart1]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ present ♥ Permanent officials and office holders. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_123_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]“It was status as an agnate [kin group or clan] in one of the noble groups that alone gave access to appointment to any state or court official of importance. Certain offices even became, with the passing of time, hereditary in a particular group”. The groups had "preferential right" to hold the office. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_124_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"There was an extensive and developed bureaucracy, as attested by ostraca from Nisa and by the Parthian parchments and ostraca from Dura-Europos."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_125_IrPart1]
♠ Source of support ♣ land ♥ Ostraca texts: "The ostraca suggest that the Parthian kings could rely on revenues in kind from their own estates."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_126_IrPart1] "Mining may also have been an important source of state revenue".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_127_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The conditions Strabo describes on the Euphrates frontier (XVI.1.27) may have prevailed elsewhere in the Parthian Empire: local strongmen collected tolls to whatever level the traffic would bear and passed as little as possible on to the royal treasury. Various types of land taxes and head tax also constituted the great bulk of all the tax revenues collected in the Sasanian Empire."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_128_IrPart1]
♠ Examination system ♣ absent ♥ Access to offices was via kin networks. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_129_IrPart1]
♠ Merit promotion ♣ absent ♥Access to offices was via kin networks. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_130_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Mithradates II rock reliefs at Behistun 87 BCE show "his principal officials ... The chief of these is called satrap of satraps, the other three simply satraps. Probably these men belonged to the great families of Iran such as the Surens and Karens."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_131_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]According to Chinese records "From Dayuan heading west towards Anxi the different countries speak different languages, but their customs are largely similar and they can understand each other’s speech. ... Women seem to be held in high respect, and the men make decisions on the advice of their women." In other words the records "suggests that the oases between the Pamirs and the Amu-darya were occupied by people who were culturally related to each other, probably all of Iranian stock.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_132_IrPart1]

Information[edit]

♠ Length ♣ inferred present ♥ Bivar (2010) says: "The principal units of length in the Near Eastern measurement systems were the foot and the cubit, standing in the ratio of 1:1.5. Subdivisions of the foot were its quarter, the palm, and its 16th, the finger. A higher unit, the fathom, consisted of four cubits."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_170_IrPart1]
♠ Area ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Volume ♣ inferred present ♥ Bivar (2010) says there was an attempt at harmonization under the Achaemenids. "The dominant units of capacity in the Achaemenid period are defined by a vessel recovered in the excavations at Persepolis. As in the case of weight-units, the reforms of Darius seem to have made an attempt to harmonize the Babylonian and Old Iranian systems."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_171_IrPart1]
♠ Weight ♣ inferred present ♥ Bivar (2010) says there was a universal standard under the Achaemenids: the standard unit of weight of the shekel "was enforced throughout the Archaemenid Empire by Darius the Great ... around 515 BCE. In this system, the shekel stood at 8.40 grams."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_172_IrPart1] "In June 2005, Simmons Gallery offered at auction a possibly unique item tentatively identified as a 'Parthian weight'." Description: "Tetradrachm, Round bronze weight with a facing face rev. flat. 20mm. 16.35gm. The weight is very close to the weight of the early Parthian Tetradrachm coins. Stylistically this looks Parthian but we have been unable to track down any comparable piece." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_173_IrPart1]
♠ Time ♣ inferred present ♥ Coins from the first century BCE were based on the Seleucid Era. "This was a lunar calendar with a month interjection to synchronize it with the Babylonian solar calendar and started in the Autumn of 312 BCE. The beginning of the year was set on first of October, although at times the Babylonian start (April 1st) was also used for coins minted in Babylonia and Seleucia-on-the-Tigris.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_174_IrPart1] There also was a "Parthian Era, starting at 247 BCE and based on the Macedonian lunar calendar" which was occasionally used in circumstances yet to be precisely specified. Also perhaps religious time-keeping: "Some ostraca found in Nisa include inscriptions which are dated using Avestan influenced month names, as opposed to the Greek ones used by the Seleucid Era, and might hint on the use of a religious calendar at least on the eastern parts of the empire."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_175_IrPart1]
♠ Geometrical ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Other ♣ ♥
♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The majority of written sources used are written in Greek and Latin, and were written by people from outside the Parthian empire looking into it. The Parthian language survives on potsherds and some graffiti.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_176_IrPart1] Lukonin comments, “The sources in the Parthian language consist mostly of fragmentary epigraphic material.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_177_IrPart1]
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ suspected unknown ♥

[MAJIDBENAM_REF_179_IrPart1] The Chinese Shiji notes "To make records they cut leather and write horizontally."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_180_IrPart1] Pahlavi parchment from Avroman in Kurdistan.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_181_IrPart1]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ "Greek served as their official language."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_182_IrPart1] "Although we know little of Parthian administrative practice we may assume it provided the basis for the Sasanian administration that followed it in a closely related form of Middle Persian, and in a related script, in the 3rd century AD."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_183_IrPart1] "After the introduction of Old Persian cuneiform under the Achaemenians, a related form of the same language (a Middle Persian language written in a simplified form of the Aramaic consonantal alphabet) continued as the vehicle of administration under the Parthians."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_184_IrPart1] Chancellery script under the Achaemenians 1st century BC. ♠ Lists, tables, and classifications ♣ present ♥ "For an insight into the forms of Parthian taxation we can now look to the ostraca found in the citadel at the royal capital of Nisa. Almost 2,500 ostraca have been found since 1948 in the former wine-storage rooms at Nisa. All the documents date from the mid-second (no. 257 is of the year 97 = 151/150 B.C.) to the late first century B.C. and record payments of rent or taxes in kind, in this case wine."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_187_IrPart1]
♠ Calendar ♣ present ♥ Evidence from Seleucia shows that the Parthians used the Babylonian system. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_188_IrPart1] "In the indigenous Parthian lands the Zoroastrian calendar was also used beside the Seleucid era."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_189_IrPart1]

♠ History ♣ present ♥ "Armenian accounts of trade routes such as Parthian Stations of the Isidore of Charax and other mentions in Armenian histories are an indication of the involvement of Armenians in the internal trade of the Arsacid Empire."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_191_IrPart1] Isidore of Charax is called a Greco-Roman but Characene was at times Parthian held territory and its literate population that we have evidence for being interested in books such as his Parthian Stations or his original work which was perhaps titled A Journey around Parthia. Characene was the area of southern Mesopotamia. "We know that varied schools of philosophy flourished in Hellenistic Babylonia, and Greek metaphysicians, astronomers, naturalists, historians, geographers, and physicians worked there. The Parthian court made considerable use of such trained and able men for building its bureaucracy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_192_IrPart1]
♠ Philosophy ♣ present ♥ "Iranians were familiar with Greek philosophy from the Achaemenid period."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_193_IrPart1] "Parthian Empire in particular seems to have become a refuge for the Jews who fled the Roman persecution and brought the fundamental of the rabbinic learning with them, to appear in the form of the Babylonian Talmud a few centuries later."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_194_IrPart1] "the Avesta (the holy book of Zoroastrianism) ... credits Vologases I (51-78 CE) with the collection and compiling of the corpus of Avesta itself. Whether true or not, this is an evidence of the interest of later Arsacid Emperors in Zoroastrianism and their possible patronage of its spread."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_195_IrPart1] "the late Arsacid period is distinguished by the rise of the aforementioned Gnosticism and Neo-Platonism. Famous leaders of Gnosticism like Marcion lived either in the Parthian territories or had a large following within their lands, while the Egyptian philosopher Plotinus made a trip to the Parthian lands".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_196_IrPart1] "We know that varied schools of philosophy flourished in Hellenistic Babylonia, and Greek metaphysicians, astronomers, naturalists, historians, geographers, and physicians worked there. The Parthian court made considerable use of such trained and able men for building its bureaucracy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_197_IrPart1]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ present ♥ "Credit for the discovery and use of the monsoon as an aid to navigation in the Indian Ocean is given to a merchant named Hippalus about the year 100 B.C."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_198_IrPart1] Astronomy, primarily for calendar.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_199_IrPart1] "We know that varied schools of philosophy flourished in Hellenistic Babylonia, and Greek metaphysicians, astronomers, naturalists, historians, geographers, and physicians worked there. The Parthian court made considerable use of such trained and able men for building its bureaucracy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_200_IrPart1]
♠ Fiction ♣ inferred present ♥ "the Arsacid rulers consciously promoted the creation of a cultural unity within their core-territories. This is best reflected in the foundation and promotion of a mythical history for Iran, possibly based on an Avestan model, and the revival of what was seen as profoundly local in nature, including literature and orthography, best marked by a return to Aramaic alphabet as the main means of communication."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_201_IrPart1] "The Hellenistic commercial aristocracy of the city [Seleucia] supported cultural endeavors, as patrons of literature, art, and academies."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_202_IrPart1] "No literature remains from the Arsacids themselves. Greek served as their official language; Greek drama was cultivated at their court ..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_203_IrPart1] Scholars assume that most Parthian literature was oral [MAJIDBENAM_REF_204_IrPart1] Mary Boyce comments, "No Parthian literature survives from the Parthian period in its original form. The only work of any length which exist in the Parthian language were composed under Sasanian rule.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_205_IrPart1] "The interest in oral literature in pre-Islamic Iran meant that, apart from state or commerical records and documents and, on rare occasions, religious works, nothing was written down until the Sasanian period. Secular oral literature was preserved orally by gosan (poet-ministrels) or khunyagar (story-tellers)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_206_IrPart1] "Epic stories, frequently in verse, remained an oral form until the Sasanian period".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_207_IrPart1] inferred present on the basis of the presence of e.g. Greek communities but for Persian tradition only it would be inferred absent.

Customers possessed checkbooks and invested in property, farms, slaves, cattle, irrigation projects, fishing, and commercial ships."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_216_IrPart1]

♠ Fastest individual communication ♣ 6 ♥ "military troops could cover little more than the 30 kilometres a day expected of ordinary travellers, but from Herodotus' statement that the postal couriers could complete the ninety-day itinerary of the Royal Road in only nine days we infer that the postal system was approximately ten times faster than military travel, couriers covering up to 300 kilometres a day."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_221_IrPart1] Susa to Ectbatana (Hamadhan) 9 days via the Cossean mountains "(XIX.19.8), a route that would have been possible for postal couriers but not, presumably, for fully-equipped soldiers".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_222_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Ctesiphon to Merv 1700 km. Couriers at 300 km per day 5.7 days. This route would not have used the Royal Road but presumably there were postal stations at many other places not on the Royal Road.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Rapid-horseman (military) 560km 3-4 days or about 160 km per day - for comparison. Is this true: using one horse?[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The combination of the horseshoe with an efficient road system, originally laid out by the Archaemenids, allowed for a rapid horseman to travel approximately 450 miles (560km) in a mere 3-4 days. It is possible that the horseshoe was already in widespread use by the battle of Carrhae in 53 BC."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_223_IrPart1]

Military Technologies[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ present ♥ bronze is made with copper: Heavy cavalry armour made from "rawhide, horn, iron, and bronze cut into scales."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_224_IrPart1] "The standard turn-out would have included helmets of bronze or iron, sometimes with a neck guard and/or an aventail of lamellar, scale or mail, sometimes sporting a small plume of horsehair, either dyed or left natural; and a corselet of lamellar, mail or scale for the torso" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_225_IrPart1]
♠ Bronze ♣ present ♥ Heavy cavalry armour made from "rawhide, horn, iron, and bronze cut into scales."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_226_IrPart1] "The standard turn-out would have included helmets of bronze or iron, sometimes with a neck guard and/or an aventail of lamellar, scale or mail, sometimes sporting a small plume of horsehair, either dyed or left natural; and a corselet of lamellar, mail or scale for the torso. Arm guards were also worn, and some wore guantlets too. The feet were often protected by armour over mail 'socks', and mail was often used to bridge defences at limb joints. A small fabric tabard and/or cloak might be worn, and this was very likely to be made of a rich material such as silk brocade." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_227_IrPart1]
♠ Iron ♣ present ♥ Iron armour.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_228_IrPart1]
♠ Steel ♣ uncertain_absent_present ♥ May have imported high quality steel. Was the Artaxerxes sword a 'trophy weapon' or representative of swords used by elite Persian forces? Could the same thing be said up until the time of the first manufacture of Damascene swords? "It is believed that Indian steel was exported in the early centuries A.D. and was known even in the time of Alexander. By the sixth century there is more definite evidence of the manufacture of Damascene swords and the steel used for this purpose came from India."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_229_IrPart1] Artaxerxes II of Persia (Achaemenids, ruled around 400 BCE) had a Greek physician called Ctesias of Cnidus who was impressed by his sword of Indian steel.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_230_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_REF_231_IrPart1] Use of Damascene steel certainly by 540 CE: "This unique type of steel was a major technological innovation and Iran played an important role in its production over the centuries. Circumstantial evidence suggests that a trade in a special steel, conceivably the ingots from which damascene steel was made, was underway in the Parthian and Sasanian period. Sometime after 115 A.D. the Parthians were importing iron (steel) from some point to the east" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_232_IrPart1] "High-carbon steel was being produced in the eastern Iranian region from the tenth century CE."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_233_IrPart1]

♠ Polearms ♣ inferred present ♥ Plutarch mentions Romans had to face "long pikes" at Carrhae.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_255_IrPart1] Are these polearms or lances? Parthians did not have much infantry so presumably these are the Parthian heavy cavalry. Cassius Dio [c. CE 155 - 235] in Roman History: “The Parthians make no use of a shield, but their forces consist of mounted archers and pikemen, mostly in full armour. Their infantry is small, made up of the weaker men; but even these are all archers. They practise from boyhood, and the climate and the land combine to aid both horsemanship and archery... They are really formidable in warfare, but nevertheless they have a reputation greater than their achievements, because in spite of their not having gained anything from the Romans, and having besides, given up certain portions of their own domain, they have not yet been enslaved, but even to this day hold their own in the wars they wage against us.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_256_IrPart1]

♠ Horses ♣ present ♥ Used by cavalry and archers on horse back. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_258_IrPart1] Our information on Parthian armies comes mainly from their enemies, especially Roman sources. Like many central Asian armies, horses were central to their war fighting, foot solders less so. Parthian cavalry was divided into heavy and light forces. The 'Parthian shot' became infamous to the Romans: Plutarch describes their tactic at the battle of Carrhae in 53 BC. Parthian cavalry pretended to flee, then turned in the saddle and fired their bow and arrows. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_259_IrPart1]
♠ Camels ♣ present ♥ Camels in supply train at Carrhae.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_260_IrPart1] "Bactrian camels began to be used for cavalry between 500 and 100 BC."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_261_IrPart1] "There is evidence that Parthian archers also used camels on occasion, which had great stamina and gave a good advantage point from which to fire, but their effectiveness was limited due to their soft feet, which would quickly become injured walking on the debris of a battlefield."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_262_IrPart1] Parthians used some archers mounted on camels.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_263_IrPart1]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ inferred present ♥Present[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Nobles had retainers and these provided military services, although mercenaries were also used. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_307_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Ctesiphon ... is said to have been built to house Parthian troops, which evidently could not be installed in Seleucia across the river."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_308_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Absent - code refers to a standing infantry army, there was a standing cavalry army.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Lacking a standing army, Parthia could never hope to arm and train an infantry to rival the disciplined phalanxes of Rome".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_309_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The infantry was composed of good quality hillmen, and of peasants who were of indifferent military worth."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_310_IrPart1]
♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ inferred absent ♥"The panoply of the cataphractarii was very expensive, and varied between individuals. Any degree of standardisation may only have been present among the royal guard and retainers of some of the greater nobles."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_311_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Because of the expense of maintaining horses and armor the heavy-armed was recruited from the nobility, who were also expected to supply and equip some light cavalry"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_312_IrPart1]
♠ Total army ♣ 300,000 ♥ "The aristocracy provided the cataphracts and trained their retainers - not their slaves - as mounted bowmen. Parthian army was small, as in most feudal states, never more than 6,000 cataphracts and 34,000 bowmen..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_313_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]20,000 "foot-soldiers" fought the Alani in 136 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_314_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"From the 1st century bc onward the Arsakids had a military potential of almost 300,000 soldiers. This mobilization strength mirrors the size of the Arsakid armed forces in a defensive stance, including the royal forces, Parthian national army, garrisons, and mercenaries. As a number of units were not suitable for offensive operations, one may assume that the power of an offensive army might not have exceeded half of the total figure, i.e. about 140,000-150,000. This is slightly more than the figure of 120,000 soldiers which appears as the total for the largest of Arsakid armies." Olbrycht, Marek J. 2016. “Manpower Resources and Army Organisation in the Arsakid Empire“. Ancient Society 46, 291-338.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_315_IrPart1]
♠ Professional army ♣ {40,000; 120,000} ♥ "The aristocracy provided the cataphracts and trained their retainers - not their slaves - as mounted bowmen. Parthian army was small, as in most feudal states, never more than 6,000 cataphracts and 34,000 bowmen..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_316_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"From the 1st century bc onward the Arsakids had a military potential of almost 300,000 soldiers. This mobilization strength mirrors the size of the Arsakid armed forces in a defensive stance, including the royal forces, Parthian national army, garrisons, and mercenaries. As a number of units were not suitable for offensive operations, one may assume that the power of an offensive army might not have exceeded half of the total figure, i.e. about 140,000-150,000. This is slightly more than the figure of 120,000 soldiers which appears as the total for the largest of Arsakid armies." Olbrycht, Marek J. 2016. “Manpower Resources and Army Organisation in the Arsakid Empire“. Ancient Society 46, 291-338.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_317_IrPart1]
♠ Largest fielded army ♣ 20,000 ♥20,000: 136 BCE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Vologeses III was said to have used an army of 20,000 in 136 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_318_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"It is part of the Christian-Syrian tradition, the so-called 'Chronicle of Arbela'. It is said in the chapter on Habel’s episcopacy (22ff. Transl. 41 Kawerau) that (the Parthian king) Vologases marched against the Persians, for a long time armed for war, with 120,000 soldiers and defeated them decisively in Khurasan after heavy fighting. However, the value of this piece of information has rightly been questioned."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_319_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Chinese Shiji records "Early on, when the Han envoys visited the kingdom of Anxi, the king of Anxi ordered 20,000 horsemen to meet them on the eastern border of his kingdom." While "The description of the 20,000 horsemen meeting him at the border sounds like an exaggeration" the number could be right in the order-of-magnitude significance of the size of army the Parthians were capable of raising. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_320_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"From the 1st century bc onward the Arsakids had a military potential of almost 300,000 soldiers. This mobilization strength mirrors the size of the Arsakid armed forces in a defensive stance, including the royal forces, Parthian national army, garrisons, and mercenaries. As a number of units were not suitable for offensive operations, one may assume that the power of an offensive army might not have exceeded half of the total figure, i.e. about 140,000-150,000. This is slightly more than the figure of 120,000 soldiers which appears as the total for the largest of Arsakid armies." Olbrycht, Marek J. 2016. “Manpower Resources and Army Organisation in the Arsakid Empire“. Ancient Society 46, 291-338.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_321_IrPart1]
♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ [170-200] ♥ Days. Speed of marching troops (Diodorus Siculus): Babylon to Susa 20 days; Susa to Persia 30 days; Susa to Ectbatana (Hamadhan) 40 days via Royal Road across Babylonia; Ectbatana to Persepolis 20 days. "These speeds are not particularly impressive: military troops could cover little more than the 30 kilometres a day expected of ordinary travellers".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_322_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]If it took 40 days via Royal Road to get from Susa to Ecbatana, which is about 400 km, it would have taken 170 days minimum to get to Merv with Ctesiphon as capital, a route that did not have a road - at least not one as good as the Royal Road. No sea passages are available for this route. This figure would be down to 57 days if we assumed a daily travel speed at the maximum of 30 km a day but the figures mentioned by Diodorus Siculus suggest 10 km per day was closer to the reality. A limitation could be presented by the stamina of oxen used in the baggage train for heavy loads (internet search suggests 5-6 miles per day whilst mules, which carried lighter loads, up to 50 miles per day).[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The combination of the horseshoe with an efficient road system, originally laid out by the Archaemenids, allowed for a rapid horseman to travel approximately 450 miles (560km) in a mere 3-4 days. It is possible that the horseshoe was already in widespread use by the battle of Carrhae in 53 BC."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_323_IrPart1] - "rapid horseman could be scout or messenger. These figures cannot be used for a whole army. Also, 160 km per day for on single horse seems excessive??
♠ Sources of recruits ♣ retainers; mercenaries ♥ ‘Knights’ given allowances of land for equipping troops at expense of a magnate. They may have been “specially trained professional warrior slaves”. Phraates employed mercenaries, a group of Sacae, in a war against the Greeks. They expected to be paid for their efforts. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_324_IrPart1]
♠ Procurement of equipment and animals ♣ by the state; by soldiers ♥ Via allowances given to regional lords. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_325_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Because of the expense of maintaining horses and armor the heavy-armed was recruited from the nobility, who were also expected to supply and equip some light cavalry, nor armored, but furnished with a bow as a weapon of offense."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_326_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Arrow supply under Mehrdad II: "A sophisticated network of workshops was established throughout Luristan, Khuzistan, Persis, Khorassan, Hyreania, and Media."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_327_IrPart1]

List of Wars[edit]

First Parthian-Seleucid (238 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Parthian-Seleucid (231 BCE-227 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Parthian-Greco-Bactrian (230 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Third Parthian-Seleucid (209 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Parthian-Greco-Bactrian (160 BCE-155 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Fourth Parthian-Seleucid (148 BCE-141 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Parthian-Sakan (140 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Not long afterwards the Parthians were for the first but not the last time forced to defend themselves against a fierce attack by nomads, possibly the Sakas, in the east. Mithridates took personal command of the campaign, even though the Seleucids were just then making ready to reconquer Mesopotamia. Presumably he considered the adversary in the east to be the more dangerous, an assessment of the situation which subsequent events confirmed as correct. The invasion in the northeast was successfully repulsed, then the Seleucid ruler Demetrius II, after making initial gains, was taken prisoner.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Fifth Parthian-Seleucid (140 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Parthian-Elymaean (139 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Sixth Parthian-Seleucid (130 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR][[]][MAJIDBENAM_BR]For their part, however, the Parthians were unable to rejoice in the victory for long because in the next few years they were again forced to come to terms with the nomads on their eastern frontier. As a result of the movements of the Huns in inner Asia various nomadic peoples began to appear in the region of the Oxus approximately during the period 133-129 BCE The most important ones were the Yüeh-chih, who conquered the Greco-Bactrian kingdom and founded the empire of the Kushans (q.v.), the Sakas, and the Massagetae who turned against the Parthian empire. (For an account of these events, see P. Daffinà, L’immigrazione dei Saka nella Drangiane, Rome, 1967.) Both Phraates II and his successor Artabanus I (ca. 127-24/3) lost their lives in the course of these struggles.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Parthian-Characenan (127 BCE-124 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Parthian-Characenan (122 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR][[]] how to name this conflict?[MAJIDBENAM_BR]then he made the northern Mesopotamian kingdoms of Adiabene, Gordyene, and Osrhoene into vassal states, and conquered Dura-Europos in 113 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Parthian-Artaxiad (110 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Parthian-Artaxiad (91 BCE-87 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Parthian-Roman (55 BCE-53 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Parthian-Roman (41 BCE-39 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Third Parthian-Roman (36 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Parthian Civil (32 BCE-25 BCE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR][[]] was this a war? worthy of being coded?[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Also, according to Josephus (Jewish Antiquities 16.253), Phraates had to contend with a further rival king by the name of Mithridates in the years 12-9 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_BR][[]] this looks like mostly a lot of posturing rather than actual war--worthy of being coded?[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Parthian nobility now turned to one of the sons of Phraates IV who had been sent to Rome. Augustus returned the eldest of them, Vonones, to Parthia where he was crowned king in 8/9. But life in Rome, in the opinion of the Parthians at least, had made Vonones “soft,” and they were unhappy about his tight budgetary control, so a rival candidate was set up by a section of the nobility. This was Artabanus who came from the northeast of Iran, probably Hyrcania. (For a comprehensive, specialist study see U. Kahrstedt, Artabanos III. und seine Erben, Bern, 1950.) When he first tried to seize power he was defeated by Vonones. Only at the second attempt was he successful, being crowned king in Ctesiphon in 10/11. Vonones withdrew to Armenia where he occupied the vacant throne for a short time, probably with Roman approval. However, when Artabanus threatened military action against him, the Romans withdrew their support from Vonones.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Encouraged by the Romans’ willingness to yield to him in this way, Artabanus now attempted to make his own son king of Armenia, but Rome was not prepared to accept this. Instead, the emperor Tiberius sent his adoptive son Germanicus to Armenia at the head of a large army, and he appointed a son of the king of Pontus as monarch there with the title Artaxes III. After this Artabanus gave way, with the result that about 18/19, amicable relations were apparently re-established on the pattern of the treaties concluded in 20 BCE and 1 BCE. The main loser was Vonones who was deported to Cilicia by the Romans and died there in CE 19 when attempting to escape.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ According to Mary Boyce [MAJIDBENAM_REF_365_IrPart1], though "[t]here is no direct contemporary evidence for the festivals celebrated by Iranians in the ill-documented Seleucid and Parthian periods [...] much can be deduced from various sources, notably the national epic, and the Zoroastrian calendar and holy books", which lead Boyce to assert that "[t]here can be no doubt that Zoroastrianism was the dominant religion in Iran in both these epochs". The largest scale collective cult for the Parthians, then, would have been Nowruz, "the most joyous and beautiful of the Zoroastrian feasts, a spring festival invested with especial religious significance. Religiously it celebrates the creation of fire, which informs all the other six creations, being as it were their spirit or life-force" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_366_IrPart1].

♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [MAJIDBENAM_REF_369_IrPart1]. Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Yasna ♥ According to Mary Boyce [MAJIDBENAM_REF_410_IrPart1], though "[t]here is no direct contemporary evidence for the festivals celebrated by Iranians in the ill-documented Seleucid and Parthian periods [...] much can be deduced from various sources, notably the national epic, and the Zoroastrian calendar and holy books", which lead Boyce to assert that "[t]here can be no doubt that Zoroastrianism was the dominant religion in Iran in both these epochs". The most widespread collective cult for the Parthians, then, would have been the Yasna ritual, the central ritual of Zoroastrianism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_411_IrPart1]. The fact that the first sacred Zoroastrian texts were compiled during Sasanian times, including the text meant to be recited during the Yasna ritual itself, also known as Yasna, is probably sufficient proof that the ritual was performed in those times. Very briefly, the ritual consisted of the sacrifice of an animal in a specially designated place, as well as the recitation of the sacred text from which the Yasna takes its name.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_412_IrPart1]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_413_IrPart1]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ daily ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_414_IrPart1]
♠ Duration ♣ [2-3] ♥ hours. two and a half hours The liturgical text from which the Yasna takes its name is recited in full during the ritual, which means that, in modern times, the ritual lasts about two and a half hours [MAJIDBENAM_REF_415_IrPart1]. This was probably about the same in ancient times.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ 2 ♥ It seems likely that only two priests presided, though it may have been eight for particularly important occasions [MAJIDBENAM_REF_416_IrPart1]. No information is available regarding the number of laypeople present at the ritual. "In the Avesta, several types of priests are listed, but according to the manuscripts, only two were involved in the yasna sacrifice, the principal, or libating, priest (zaotar) and his assistant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_417_IrPart1] "There is no specific term for the assistant (he is called raspi, from unattestd Avestan rathwiya, 'the original one in charge of the ratus'), and he speaks only to answer the libating priest. It is the libating priest who conducted all the formal rituals".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_418_IrPart1]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ priests. It seems likely that only two priests presided, though it may have been eight for particularly important occasions [MAJIDBENAM_REF_419_IrPart1]. Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Yasna ♥ According to Mary Boyce [MAJIDBENAM_REF_484_IrPart1], though "[t]here is no direct contemporary evidence for the festivals celebrated by Iranians in the ill-documented Seleucid and Parthian periods [...] much can be deduced from various sources, notably the national epic, and the Zoroastrian calendar and holy books", which lead Boyce to assert that "[t]here can be no doubt that Zoroastrianism was the dominant religion in Iran in both these epochs". The most widespread collective cult for the Parthians, then, would have been the Yasna ritual is the central ritual of Zoroastrianism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_485_IrPart1]. The fact that the first sacred Zoroastrian texts were compiled during Sasanian times, including the text meant to be recited during the Yasna ritual itself, also known as Yasna, is probably sufficient proof that the ritual was performed in those times. Very briefly, the ritual consisted of the sacrifice of an animal in a specially designated place, as well as the recitation of the sacred text from which the Yasna takes its name.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_486_IrPart1]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_487_IrPart1]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ daily ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_488_IrPart1]
♠ Duration ♣ [2-3] ♥ hours. two and a half hours The liturgical text from which the Yasna takes its name is recited in full during the ritual, which means that, in modern times, the ritual lasts about two and a half hours [MAJIDBENAM_REF_489_IrPart1]. This was probably about the same in ancient times.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ 2 ♥ It seems likely that only two priests presided, though it may have been eight for particularly important occasions [MAJIDBENAM_REF_490_IrPart1]. No information is available regarding the number of laypeople present at the ritual. "In the Avesta, several types of priests are listed, but according to the manuscripts, only two were involved in the yasna sacrifice, the principal, or libating, priest (zaotar) and his assistant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_491_IrPart1] "There is no specific term for the assistant (he is called raspi, from unattestd Avestan rathwiya, 'the original one in charge of the ratus'), and he speaks only to answer the libating priest. It is the libating priest who conducted all the formal rituals".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_492_IrPart1]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ According to Mary Boyce [MAJIDBENAM_REF_558_IrPart1], though "[t]here is no direct contemporary evidence for the festivals celebrated by Iranians in the ill-documented Seleucid and Parthian periods [...] much can be deduced from various sources, notably the national epic, and the Zoroastrian calendar and holy books", which lead Boyce to assert that "[t]here can be no doubt that Zoroastrianism was the dominant religion in Iran in both these epochs". The largest scale collective cult for the Parthians, then, would have been Nowruz, "the most joyous and beautiful of the Zoroastrian feasts, a spring festival invested with especial religious significance. Religiously it celebrates the creation of fire, which informs all the other six creations, being as it were their spirit or life-force" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_559_IrPart1].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [MAJIDBENAM_REF_560_IrPart1].

♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [MAJIDBENAM_REF_562_IrPart1]. Thomas Cressy: As the ritual is described as having 'mandatory participation', it seems there is no evidence of passive spectators. Coded as 'not applicable' instead of 'yearly'

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Zoroastrian mortuary ritual ♥ The Zoroastrian ritual of exposing cadavers to the elements, and subsequently to gather the bones and deposit them in ossuaries, is believed to have become "more or less obligatory" under Sasanid rule [MAJIDBENAM_REF_603_IrPart1]. Indeed, the most detailed ancient account of this practice dates to Sasanid times: sixth-century CE Byzantine historian Agathias wrote "this is the Persian funeral practice: the flesh is removed in this way and the sposed bones rot, scattered at random all over the plain. They consider it irreligious to place the dead in a tomb or container, or even to bury them in the ground" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_604_IrPart1]. Sacred Zoroastrian texts instruct that "[t]he corpse is to be exposed naked, and must be bound to the ground or fastened to it with stones or bricks. The flesh must then be consumed swiftly by birds and dogs" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_605_IrPart1]. Indeed, outside observers in ancient times often make specific reference to the dogs and birds to which the Iranian dead were destined [MAJIDBENAM_REF_606_IrPart1]. This ritual is classified here as the most dysphoric collective ritual because dead bodies appear to have elicited feelings of fear and disgust in the participants: "Death being regarded as an evil brought about by Aŋra Mainyu (Ahriman), the Destructive Spirit, the corpse of a holy creature, particularly man or dog, is considered to be greatly infested by the druj Nasu, the creature of the Lie called Corpse-matter. Burial of “corpse matter” (nasa-niganih) would defile the earth (in the Sad dar, chap. 33, it is said that Spandārmad shudders when a corpse is buried) and was therefore prohibited" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_607_IrPart1].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ in terms of whole polity "daily" in terms of a particular ossuary possibly weekly? frequent Inferred from the fact that this ritual was performed every time somebody died.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Dysphoric elements

♠ fear ♣ present ♥ Inferred from the following quote: "Death being regarded as an evil brought about by Aŋra Mainyu (Ahriman), the Destructive Spirit, the corpse of a holy creature, particularly man or dog, is considered to be greatly infested by the druj Nasu, the creature of the Lie called Corpse-matter. Burial of “corpse matter” (nasa-niganih) would defile the earth (in the Sad dar, chap. 33, it is said that Spandārmad shudders when a corpse is buried) and was therefore prohibited" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_610_IrPart1].

Burial of “corpse matter” (nasā-nigānīh) would defile the earth (in the Sad dar, chap. 33, it is said that Spandārmad shudders when a corpse is buried) and was therefore prohibited" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_612_IrPart1].

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ disputed_absent_present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_639_IrPart1] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_640_IrPart1], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_641_IrPart1] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[MAJIDBENAM_REF_642_IrPart1] and priests[MAJIDBENAM_REF_643_IrPart1]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_644_IrPart1] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_645_IrPart1] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_646_IrPart1] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_647_IrPart1] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_648_IrPart1] 'Would the Council of the Nobles, which drew and enacted the laws, and had an important role in the succession especially in the later Parthian era[MAJIDBENAM_REF_649_IrPart1], have had to agree to the military use of force? One might speculate this was the case in theory but the king could do his own thing when opinion in the Mahistan was split.'
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ inferred present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_650_IrPart1] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_651_IrPart1], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_652_IrPart1] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[MAJIDBENAM_REF_653_IrPart1] and priests[MAJIDBENAM_REF_654_IrPart1]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_655_IrPart1] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_656_IrPart1] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_657_IrPart1] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_658_IrPart1] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_659_IrPart1] The Council of the Nobles drew and enacted the laws. In any case, most lawmaking would have been done at local levels by the autonomous cities and vassal states.

The Parthian aristocracy reflected in the Mahistan (Council of Nobles) had the power to draw and enact law and especially in the later Parthian era appoint the king's successor.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_711_IrPart1] However, the sources do not say that the Mahistan could punish the king. The culture implied by a hypothetical Council punishing a king is at odds with the observation the Parthian king was primus inter pares with the powerful lords. Disagreements between the Parthian king and the nobles would more likely have broken down into fighting rather than ended in a legal action which would have attempted to re-establish the power of the lords; Parthian history particularly from the 1st century BCE charts the existence of open conflicts between rulers and aristocrats.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_712_IrPart1]

Given the high level of local autonomy, opposition to the executive was usually expressed through provincial revolts so the non-government (i.e. powerful families) did not need to sanction the executive to get their way.

Local government also proved to be a constraint: autonomy granted to Greek cities such as Seleucia on the Tigris "often the source of tensions and serious conflicts with Parthian rulers trying to restrain it."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_734_IrPart1] The Parthians provided the autonomous city of Susa with its own charter.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_735_IrPart1]

Elite families such as the Surens and Karens had the greatest influence and probably held top posts such as "satrap of satraps" and were regular satraps.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_746_IrPart1] The reason why they held the top posts was presumably because there would have been unwanted consequences for the king had they not have been able to retain them. The interests of the elite families were not always identical with that of the king's. "As the nobility settled down, and intermarried with the old upper classes, local loyalties took the place of former dynastic ones."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_747_IrPart1]
