These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_765_IrPart1] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_766_IrPart1] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_767_IrPart1] The Parthian king was considered first among equals within the aristocracy. He likely would have received informal advice from members of aristocratic families beyond the remit of the posts they may have held at the Parthian court.
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_768_IrPart1] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_769_IrPart1], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_770_IrPart1] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[MAJIDBENAM_REF_771_IrPart1] and priests[MAJIDBENAM_REF_772_IrPart1]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_773_IrPart1] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_774_IrPart1] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_775_IrPart1] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_776_IrPart1] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_777_IrPart1] The Parthian aristocracy working through the Mahistan (Council of Nobles)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_778_IrPart1] was part of the government. However, the aristocrats could also have used informal channels to influence the king. However the culture implied by aristocrats agreeing to punish a Parthian king through any mechanism is at odds with the observation the Parthian king was primus inter pares with the powerful lords. Disagreements between the Parthian king and the nobles would more likely have broken down into fighting rather than ended in a legal action which would have attempted to re-establish the power of the lords; Parthian history particularly from the 1st century BCE charts the existence of open conflicts between rulers and aristocrats.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_779_IrPart1]
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ inferred present ♥ "The king stood at the head of the state, but royal power was regarded as the collective property of the Arsacid family ... a principle that led to rival claimants and frequently involved dynastic conflicts."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_780_IrPart1] The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_781_IrPart1] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_782_IrPart1], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_783_IrPart1] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[MAJIDBENAM_REF_784_IrPart1] and priests[MAJIDBENAM_REF_785_IrPart1]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_786_IrPart1] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_787_IrPart1] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_788_IrPart1] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_789_IrPart1] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_790_IrPart1] The Parthian king could not appoint whom he pleased due to the influence of the powerful families who may well have used both formal (Council of Nobles) and informal mechanisms to influence the king. It is known there were vested interests in appointments to office: "Certain offices even became, with the passing of time, hereditary in a particular group." The groups had "preferential right" to hold the office.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_791_IrPart1]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". ♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ present ♥ The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[MAJIDBENAM_REF_823_IrPart1] and priests[MAJIDBENAM_REF_824_IrPart1]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_825_IrPart1] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_826_IrPart1] "We have no information about the place occupied at the court of the Parthian ruler by the Zoroastrian priests, since the part played by Zoroastrianism in the Parthian state has not been entirely clarified."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_827_IrPart1] "Parthian respect for the rights of priests and the autonomy of temples in Babylonia."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_828_IrPart1] "the strange Parthian law of succession. According to Lukonin (1983) it is not known what role the dominant religion, Zoroastrianism, had in the Parthian state. However we can code present because the Parthians had to respect the "rights of priests and the autonomy of temples in Babylonia."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_832_IrPart1]

♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ ♥ unknown "We have no information about the place occupied at the court of the Parthian ruler by the Zoroastrian priests, since the part played by Zoroastrianism in the Parthian state has not been entirely clarified."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_851_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Customary mechanism ♣ present ♥ Since Arsaces I "the Parthian state was an absolute inherited monarchy with a traditional principle of passing down the rule to the eldest son of the ruling monarch." ♠ Legal mechanism ♣ inferred absent: 247-53 BCE; unknown: 54 BCE - 40 CE ♥ Since Arsaces I "the Parthian state was an absolute inherited monarchy with a traditional principle of passing down the rule to the eldest son of the ruling monarch." However, there was a large degree of variation as Parthian kings also appointed sons as corulers, or might name as his successor his brother.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_855_IrPart1] "The king was traditionally elected by the nobles from the members of the Arsacid family, although the succession of the eldest son was not guaranteed."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_856_IrPart1] "the strange Parthian law of succession. Armenia and Adiabene followed the Arsacid pattern."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_857_IrPart1] We know dynastic change could be influenced by the opinion of the nobles. Earlier Parthian periods can be coded absent on the basis that it was especially in the later Parthian era that the Mahistan could choose the king.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_858_IrPart1]

It is precisely in this period that Sistan gains an independent or semi-independent status, for the coinage attest to the existence of two rulers, Maues (98/85 BCE - 60/75 BCE) and Vovones (75 BCE - 57 BCE), one predominantly in the east and one in the west, minting coins and giving themselves the title of king of kings."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_865_IrPart1] Dabrowa says the crisis which began while Mithradates II was alive with the c92 BCE challenge of usurper Gotarzes I was over by 88 CE on the latter's death: "The crisis situation was only overcome by Sinatruces ... who ascended to the Arsacid throne ca. 88/87 BCE."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_866_IrPart1] *79* There must be expert disagreement here or else the preceding 88/87 BCE date was a typo as in the chronology of the same book Sinatruces succeeded Orodes I although here it is suggested Philopator briefly succeeded Orodes I: "About 79 BC, Orodes I was driven from Babylon. There may have been a brief succession by a son of Gotarzes known only as Philopator from his coinage. Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥The Parthians consisted of a "a military aristocracy" over a decentralized Empire of many peoples whom already had possession of extensive administration inherited from earlier states which the Parthians did not destroy.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_877_IrPart1] The Parthians had some centralized institutions themselves but these were not based at a capital city but "moved from city to city along with their administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_878_IrPart1] The state bureaucracy was a royal court bureaucracy "copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwāns, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_879_IrPart1] The Diwans "were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_880_IrPart1] Highly-literate administrators were regularly recruited from the pool of talent that existed within the territory of the Empire, particularly Hellenistic Babylonia.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_881_IrPart1] So much so, the official language of Parthian administration was Greek[MAJIDBENAM_REF_882_IrPart1], although Middle Persian may also have been in use[MAJIDBENAM_REF_883_IrPart1] and the azat (free people) who represented "descendents of the nomadic aristocracy of Parni ... filled the top posts in the state administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_884_IrPart1] While the apparently peripatetic bureaucracy "is very poorly documented"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_885_IrPart1] there were administrative achievements in the form of public works in Parthian regions, such as the road network which the Arsacids maintained and expanded "to the north-east to include their major cities of Rhagae and Nisa."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_886_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR][MAJIDBENAM_REF_887_IrPart1]
♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ lower ♥The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_888_IrPart1] The Parthians had some centralized institutions but these were not based at a capital city but "moved from city to city along with their administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_889_IrPart1] The state bureaucracy was a royal court bureaucracy "copied from the Achaemenid model".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_890_IrPart1] Additionally at the top of the Parthian state there was a Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_891_IrPart1] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[MAJIDBENAM_REF_892_IrPart1] and priests[MAJIDBENAM_REF_893_IrPart1]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_894_IrPart1] There were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_895_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]At the central government the Parthian aristocracy had more prestige than the administrators in the royal court bureaucracy because the Mahistan had an important role in lawmaking and because it was Parthian, whereas the bureaucracy would have contained non-Parthians such as Greeks. There also would have been a military and possibly religious aspect to this prestige since the Mahistan contained generals and priests. Furthermore within the Parthian Empire the royal court bureaucracy was probably less prestigious than the aristocrats because the majority of administrative and governance activity was legally conducted at the level of the provinces and cities, which were given a lot of autonomy (even rights enshrined in charter[MAJIDBENAM_REF_896_IrPart1]). Compounding this the royal court bureaucracy was seen more as the personal organ of a Parthian king than an indispensable institution of state. "As the nobility settled down, and intermarried with the old upper classes, local loyalties took the place of former dynastic ones."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_897_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Authority over state-level administrators
♠ Direct ♣ present ♥ The Parthians had some centralized institutions themselves but these were not based at a capital city but "moved from city to city along with their administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_898_IrPart1] The state bureaucracy was a royal court bureaucracy "copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwāns, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_899_IrPart1] The Diwans "were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_900_IrPart1] Given the close association of the Parthian bureaucracy to the king - that is, within his court, his authority over them would have been direct.

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ 18: 1-40 CE ♥18: 1-100 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"In the first century CE, Parthia was divided into eighteen satrapies".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_923_IrPart1] "Part of the kingdom was divided into satrapies ruled by satraps appointed by the king. The rest consisted of vassal kingdoms."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_924_IrPart1]
♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥Parthain provinces known as satrapi were ruled by governors who were the top administrator and the highest military official.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_925_IrPart1] "In the first century CE, Parthia was divided into eighteen satrapies".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_926_IrPart1] Elite families such as the Surens and Karens had the greatest influence and probably held top posts such as "satrap of satraps" and were regular satraps.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_927_IrPart1] "In some cases power over a number of satrapies (usually along the frontiers) was concentrated in the hands of the same person."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_928_IrPart1] "The various parts of their realm rule by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_929_IrPart1] The provincial administration was "extensive" and staffed by specialized officials at multiple levels who were responisble for the collection of duties and taxes and supervised local communities and administrative units.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_930_IrPart1] The well-developed nature of the provincial bureaucracy is attested by ostraca from Parthian Nisa and also Parthian parchments and ostraca recovered at Dura-Europos.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_931_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]However, the Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities, each appropriate to the area governed, to whom they maintained a feudal relationship and to whose subjects they desired little direct relationship at all."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_932_IrPart1] This lead to "provincial government ... divided into two systems. The first was the system of the client kingdoms and the second, provinces or satrapis ran much like the Achaemenid model. The client kingdoms of the Arsacid Empire, mainly clustered in the western and southern portions of the empire, were ruled by local dynasties and were considered personal clients of the Arsacid king. These had a high degree of internal autonomy and administrative freedom, often being only responsible for paying an annual tribute to the court."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_933_IrPart1] Their "normal tendency [was] to preserve local administrative practices in the conquered area"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_934_IrPart1] "Source information suggests that typically they used for their own administrative purposes any organization structures already existing in captured territories. In this respect, the Arsacids pursued a policy resembling that of the Seleucids, who built their administration on Achaemenid structures."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_935_IrPart1] The geography of the provincial administration of the Parthian Empire can be divided into three areas: self governing kingdoms in Mesopotamia; semi-independent kingdoms on the borders of Iran); and Parthian 'heartlands' in the centre under rule of the King.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_936_IrPart1] Greek city-states "became increasingly oligarchical, popular assemblies declined in significance, and power was concentrated in the hands of a council made up of representatives of a few of the richest families."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_937_IrPart1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Authority over local-level administrators
♠ Direct ♣ inferred present: 247-53 BCE; uncertain_absent_present: 52 BCE - 40 CE ♥ There is no data available on what administration was like in cities that were established by Parthians.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_938_IrPart1] Nisa/Mithradatocarta, Hecatompylos, Vologasocerta and Ctesiphon are examples of cities founded by the Parthians.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_939_IrPart1] The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities, each appropriate to the area governed, to whom they maintained a feudal relationship and to whose subjects they desired little direct relationship at all."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_940_IrPart1] "Different from the client kingdoms were the satrapis, ruled by governors appointed by the central state. We can only guess that initially, these satraps were fully responsible to the Emperor. However, with the increasing decentralization of the Arsacid state and the ever increasing power of the local land-owners and members of the Arsacid nobility, the government of the satrapi was also reduced to a form of vassal rulership, although probably with a more strict taxation system that could be more easily redrawn in comparison with that of the client kingdoms."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_941_IrPart1] We do know that while the apparently peripatetic bureaucracy "is very poorly documented"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_942_IrPart1] there were administrative achievements in the form of public works in Parthian regions, such as the road network which the Arsacids maintained and expanded "to the north-east to include their major cities of Rhagae and Nisa."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_943_IrPart1] While the central government wasn't extensive and direct rule wasn't normally sought a Parthian king may have at times exercised direct authority as and when required, for example to found new cities or ensure building projects or public works were carried out. Direct rule would have been possible due to the fact the bureaucracy moved with the king's court. Where the king went his bureaucracy was there to ensure his orders were carried out.

Status[edit]

Elite status
♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ "It was status as an agnate [kin group or clan] in one of the noble groups that alone gave access to appointment to any state or court official of importance. Certain offices even became, with the passing of time, hereditary in a particular group." The groups had "preferential right" to hold the office.[1,007] Elite families such as the Surens and Karens had the greatest influence and probably held top posts such as "satrap of satraps" and were regular satraps.[1,008]
♠ elite status is selected ♣ present ♥ Highly-literate administrators were regularly recruited from the pool of talent that existed within the territory of the Empire, particularly Hellenistic Babylonia.[1,009] So much so, the official language of Parthian administration was Greek[1,010], although Middle Persian may also have been in use.[1,011]

♠ occupational mobility ♣ present ♥ "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to chose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[1,018]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ [10-25] ♥ Perhaps in Greek run cities but not in territories directly run by the Parthians, except for war captives.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Although slaves must have constituted a large social group, little can be discerned about their place and role in the Parthian society."[1,019][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"In the eastern regions, the switch to a class society only took place at the beginning of the first millennium B.C. In that and particularly in the Achaemenid period, slavery became a widespread practice in the eastern Iranian regions." [1,020] However, not in the Parthian regions: the lower class Parni were "dependent on the Parni aristocracy."[1,021] The sedentary agriculturalists "were formed into communities, where they personally enjoyed full legal rights. They owned specific property, which they could buy and sell. But they were not considered entirely free, and there was a clear distinction between them and the upper class. They depended on the ruling class, and that depedence was collective. ... in no case could they obtain full personal freedom. Exploitation of that social class was apparent and primarily took the form of payment of taxes."[1,022] This exploitation is not strictly slavery.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Written sources do not seem to indicate the existence of slavery outside military slavery in the Parthian lands. War captives were often used as free labor and there are several mentions of major construction projects being undertaken using war captives."[1,023][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to choose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[1,024][MAJIDBENAM_BR]However, slavery surely was present in the Greek city Seleucia on the Tigris - and at Susa.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The major slave-holding centres were the Greek city-states. Manumission records found in Susa reflect the considerable extent of ancient slavery."[1,025]
♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ 0 ♥ "The Parthian society was essentially divided into two division of land-holding and landless population."[1,026] "Small land-owners were consisted of village chiefs and petty farmers. These usually wielded much local power and often acted as agents of the nobility in managing their lands as well. Land was thus rented to the peasants who were entitled to the products of their labor and had to pay a rent for the use of the agricultural land."[1,027] "In general, the populations of the rural and agricultural areas were the semi-land owning or tenant farmers who produced various agricultural products and used them to pay rents while keeping their claims to the land and guaranteeing themselves a freedom of movement and sale of excess products."[1,028] Were rural peasants legally 'bound to the land' which is the definition of serfdom or merely renting it from the lords? Seems like they were 'guaranteed freedom of movement' so were not bound. "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to choose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[1,029] It seems as though it would have been possible for a landless peasant to move to a city and pursue an alternative occupation there.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]In Parthian regions the sedentary agriculturalists "were formed into communities, where they personally enjoyed full legal rights. Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the fact that rulers were legitimated by the gods both among the Achaemenids and among the Sasanians. Schmitt says that although the Achaemenid kings were not gods or descendants of gods, 'the other fundamental basis of their kingship beside the genealogical principle is the theory of divine right of kings, Gottesgnadentum. They are kings vasna Auramazdaha, "by the favor of Ahura Mazda;" [...]. This supreme god "bestowed the empire" (xsacam frabara) on the kings. As these and similar expressions show, the Achaemenids' reign is legitimized by the gods, and the king is invested by them; i.e., he is their elect and their representative on earth [...]' [1,052] As for the Sasanians: "As seen on Ardashir’s investiture relief above, for Sasanian monarchs it was all about the xwarrah, the divine fortune or glory, which was brought to the king, and then to the land and its inhabitants through the bestowal of power by Ohrmazd." [1,053] Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.
♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that, for both the Achaemenids [1,054] and the Sasanians [1,055], the rulers benefited from supernatural energy (known as xwarrah, farnah and other variants), but did not control it--Ahura Mazda, the Zoroastrian god, did. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.
♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this was not believed to be the case either among the Achaemenids or among the Sasanians. "The Achaemenid kings were no gods ... and they were not of divine origin." [1,056] As for the Sasanians, their iconography and inscriptions make it quite clear that the rulers are blessed by the gods [1,057], but have not descended from them. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.
♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this was not believed to be the case either among the Achaemenids or among the Sasanians. Despite the implication in several Greek and Roman sources that the Persian kings were worshipped as gods, 'there is in fact no evidence that they were even deified after death ... and Darius' inscriptions show that deification did not enter his thoughts. In Egypt a Pharaoh was divine. But in the Near East, as in Greece, the notion was - and for 1,500 years had been - universally rejected as long as divinity was regarded as supernal'.[1,058] As for the Sasanians, though the privileged relationship between rulers and gods is a common subject in Sasanian iconography and inscriptions [1,059], nothing in the sources indicates that rulers became gods after death. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.
♠ Automatic deification ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this was not believed to be the case either among the Achaemenids or among the Sasanians. Despite the implication in several Greek and Roman sources that the Persian kings were worshipped as gods, 'there is in fact no evidence that they were even deified after death ... and Darius' inscriptions show that deification did not enter his thoughts. In Egypt a Pharaoh was divine. But in the Near East, as in Greece, the notion was - and for 1,500 years had been - universally rejected as long as divinity was regarded as supernal'.[1,060] As for the Sasanians, though the privileged relationship between rulers and gods is a common subject in Sasanian iconography and inscriptions [1,061], nothing in the sources indicates that rulers became gods after death. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.
♠ Rulers are gods ♣ disputed_absent_present ♥ Inferred from the fact that this was not believed to be the case either among the Achaemenids or among the Sasanians. 'The Achaemenid kings were no gods, in spite of Aeschylus (Persae 157), where Queen Atossa is called "a god's [Darius'] wife" and "a god's [Xerxes'] mother" (here and elsewhere Greek ideas are brought forward); and they were not of divine origin.'[1,062] As for the Sasanians, their iconography and inscriptions make it quite clear that the rulers are blessed by the gods [1,063], but are not gods themselves. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ inferred absent ♥ Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [1,064]. At the same time, a passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,065]
♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,066]
♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ absent ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,067]
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ inferred present ♥ "According to Josephus, Cyrus II had been ‘moved by God’ to encourage the Jews to return home and to rebuild the temple in Jerusalem (Antiquities 11.1.1- 2). This perception of Ancient Persian rule as instrumental in the unfolding of Jewish religious history had endured well into the Parthian period, where such beneficence contrasted sharply with the destruction wrought by the Romans. Jewish chronicles from the Middle Ages mention the Parthian period as one of the best in their history, during which the Jews maintained close and positive contacts with the ruling Iranians." [1,068]
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ disputed_absent_present ♥ There is disagreement about the degree of sexual equality in Zoroastrianism. According to more ancient Indo-Iranian religious traditions, women could not hope for 'ascent to paradise' but were consigned, along with slaves, to 'the kingdom of shadows beneath the earth'.[1,069] Ketayun Gould thinks that Zoroastrian teachings then 'challenged the status quo by introducing a more gender-inclusive philosophy'. 'Paradise was no longer the exclusive domain of men who had the means to achieve it'.[1,070] Similarly, 'Mary Boyce and Albert de Jong argued that women were generally held in high esteem and occupied a dignified position in Zoroastrianism, their negative portrayal within the same tradition being only isolated and occasional occurrences and in any case inconsistent with Zoroastrian teaching'.[1,071] However, Jamsheed Choksy's book 'Evil, Good, and Gender' challenged this widespread opinion, pulling together malign images of women and femininity (e.g. female demons) from a variety of Zoroastrian texts and arguing that these symbolic structures would have had direct implications for women's daily lives.[1,072] Rose describes how Zoroastrian concerns with ritual purity made 'all women of childbearing age' (along with male priests and male corpse-bearers) into 'liminal figures'; their 'lives have also been constantly circumscribed due to their biological production of "dead matter" through menstruation or childbirth'.[1,073] The position of women in this religious tradition remains 'paradoxical' because 'In the past, the notion of gender equality in terms of the spiritual potential of women has been tempered by the regular exclusion of women from the ritual domain, due to their particular physiology'.[1,074]
♠ Ideological thought equates people of all locations ♣ ♥
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes rulers and commoners ♣ present ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,075]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ present ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,084]
♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that polygamy was practiced by both Achaemenid [1,085] and Sasanian [1,086] elites. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it is also reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.
♠ Ideological thought reinforces prosociality ♣ present ♥ In an article that stresses continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism, Boyce describes offerings to the dead and the rituals connected with these among modern worshippers: 'Many of these rituals are accompanied by offerings of food and drink, which are afterwards partaken of by the living in communion with the dead, the soul being invited back to join its kinsmen and friends, not in grief but in companionable happiness. In Irani idiom such observances are for "the rejoicing of the soul" ... Some of the offerings are also given to the poor, so that piety towards the dead, cheerfulness, and charity are combined in a way that is wholly Zoroastrian.'[1,087] Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[1,088] "Records found in excavations at Nisa provide evidence of different types of tax collection ... There are also indications of the existence of special levies for the support of religious activities, somewhat similar to tithes."[1,089]
♠ charity ♣ present ♥ In an article that stresses continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism, Boyce describes offerings to the dead and the rituals connected with these among modern worshippers: 'Many of these rituals are accompanied by offerings of food and drink, which are afterwards partaken of by the living in communion with the dead, the soul being invited back to join its kinsmen and friends, not in grief but in companionable happiness. In Irani idiom such observances are for "the rejoicing of the soul" ... Some of the offerings are also given to the poor, so that piety towards the dead, cheerfulness, and charity are combined in a way that is wholly Zoroastrian.'[1,090] Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[1,091]
♠ production of public goods ♣ inferred present ♥ Balali, Keulartz and Korthals link Zoroastrian ethics to the construction of qanats, an ancient irrigation system, in pre-Islamic Iran. Through the Zoroastrian exhortation to carry out 'good deeds', they argue that the worshipper 'is directed to relieve the poor, to irrigate and cultivate the soil, to provide food and fresh water in places where needed, and to devote the surplus of his wealth in charity to the well-being and prosperity of his fellow man'.[1,092] However, the focus of their article is on the lessons we could learn from 'traditional' systems of water management, rather than on rigorous historical analysis. It is unclear to what extent worshippers of Ahura Mazda in this specific polity actually laboured to produce public goods, directed solely by their religious values.
♠ communal dining ♣ present ♥ In an article that stresses continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism, Boyce describes offerings to the dead and the rituals - including communal dining - connected with these among modern worshippers: 'Many of these rituals are accompanied by offerings of food and drink, which are afterwards partaken of by the living in communion with the dead, the soul being invited back to join its kinsmen and friends, not in grief but in companionable happiness. In Irani idiom such observances are for "the rejoicing of the soul"'.[1,093] "Several feasts and festivals are mentioned in textual sources. Spring equinox or the Norouz seems to have been a prominent festival, although not marking the beginning of the year as in later eras. Other harvest related festivals are always mentioned while the Babylonian Astronomical Diaries mention the continuation of old Babylonian and Mesopotamian celebrations, although these might have had only a local acceptance."[1,094]
♠ morality ♣ present ♥ "The individual is encouraged always to choose that which is good in terms of thought, word and action (1.30.2). This choice is encapsulated in the trifold ethic expressed as humata, hukhta, hvareshta - ‘good thoughts, good words, good deeds’ (YH 36.5, 35.2), which remains a seminal manthra for Zoroastrians." [1,095]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Slavery[edit]

♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-ethnics ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the fact that one could become enslaved through debt. "Judging by conditions before and after the Parthian period, the lowest groups on the social scale comprised slaves of various kinds. Perpetual warfare must have ensured an ample supply of foreign captives (*wartak, *anaxsahrik, Arm. anasxarhik [Perikhanian, 1973, p. 435]; see BARDA AND BARDADARi) who could be put to work in agriculture and construction. Slaves who worked the mines of the Parthian king were branded (Perikhanian, Camb. Hist. Iran, p. 635). Another type of slavery resulted from indebtedness or insolvency; a fort of indentureship similar to Sasanian bandag (see below) is attested by a loan contract from Dura Europos (parchment no. 10; Rostovtzeff and Welles, pp. 46-47), in which it was stipulated that, as interest on the loan, the debtor, a peasant, was to serve the creditor, an aristocrat, as a slave (see CONTRACTS ii; Frye, p. 187)." [1,096]
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-religionists ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the fact that one could become enslaved through debt. "Judging by conditions before and after the Parthian period, the lowest groups on the social scale comprised slaves of various kinds. Perpetual warfare must have ensured an ample supply of foreign captives (*wartak, *anaxsahrik, Arm. anasxarhik [Perikhanian, 1973, p. 435]; see BARDA AND BARDADARi) who could be put to work in agriculture and construction. Slaves who worked the mines of the Parthian king were branded (Perikhanian, Camb. Hist. Iran, p. 635). Another type of slavery resulted from indebtedness or insolvency; a fort of indentureship similar to Sasanian bandag (see below) is attested by a loan contract from Dura Europos (parchment no. 10; Rostovtzeff and Welles, pp. 46-47), in which it was stipulated that, as interest on the loan, the debtor, a peasant, was to serve the creditor, an aristocrat, as a slave (see CONTRACTS ii; Frye, p. 187)." [1,097]
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery universally ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that slaves were a part of Parthian society. "Judging by conditions before and after the Parthian period, the lowest groups on the social scale comprised slaves of various kinds. Perpetual warfare must have ensured an ample supply of foreign captives (*wartak, *anaxsahrik, Arm. anasxarhik [Perikhanian, 1973, p. 435]; see BARDA AND BARDADARi) who could be put to work in agriculture and construction. Slaves who worked the mines of the Parthian king were branded (Perikhanian, Camb. Hist. Iran, p. 635). ♠ Influential figure forbids human sacrifice for in-group member ♣ ♥ An ‘influential figure’ can be an ideological (religious) leader, e.g. a Jewish prophet, Chinese sage. Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)
♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ polytheistic ♥ "Zarathustra had a particularly close relationship with Ahura Mazdā, from whom he received revelatory visions (daēnā-). His vision, expressed in the Gathas, included a radical transformation of traditional beliefs. In place of the pantheon he elevated Ahura Mazdā to a position of supremacy that approaches monotheism and surrounded him with a group of abstract entities, the Aməša Spəntas, all of whom perpetuate key concepts of Iranian religion as hypostases of Ahura Mazdā."[1,112]
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ present ♥ "The name of the supreme God of the Zoroastrian faith is Ahura Mazda (or Ohrmazd). He is self-created, omniscient, omnipresent, holy, invisible, and beyond human conceptualization (Yasna 4.7, 29.4, 31.13, 45.5; Yasht 1.7, 1.8, 1.12, 1.13-14; Videvdat 2.1, 19.20)."[1,113]
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ inferred present ♥ 'Ahura-Mazda' originally meant 'the all-knowing Lord', but by Achaemenid times had become simply a name.[1,114] However, the quality of divine omniscience seems to have been retained: Whitley uses Yasna 29 to assert that in Zoroastrianism, 'the universe, man and mind are alike created and sustained by Ahura Mazda, omniscient of past, present and future'.[1,115] Shaki also refers to the 'omniscient Mazdean religion'.[1,116]
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ absent ♥ "In taking the immense step of seeing Ahura Mazdā as God, rather than only as one of the great gods of the Iranian pantheon, Zoroaster stopped short of conceiving him as omnipotent. According to his revelation, suffering and evil in this world, and torment in the next, have a source other than Ahura Mazdā. He can diminish and in the end annihilate them, but not control or direct them in the present."[1,117]
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ moralizing ♥ The following quote makes it clear the Zoroastrian god, Ahura Mazda, was both responsible for the creation of the world and for punishing transgressors. "[T]he prophet feels also the awe due to the God who is “Creator of all” (Y. 44.7), and who, as Lord of aša, presides over a strict justice for mankind by which the wicked, who have chosen to act “at Lie’s commands” (Y. 31.1) will be in due course condemned. Their destination is the Worst Existence, i.e., Hell, which has been brought into being by Angra Mainyu (Y. 30.4). Mazdā has “appointed recompenses” (Y. 43.5), decreeing that “the end shall be different for each” (Y. 48.4); but he “has left to men’s wills (the choice between) holy and unholy” (Y. 45.9), and it is their own inner selves (daēnā-) which lead sinners to destruction (Y. 51.13)."[1,118] [MAJIDBENAM_BR]Supernatural Enforcement of Morality[MAJIDBENAM_BR]For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Include specific details of the nature and extent of punishment/reward in the description. Code “compound” moral actions (e.g., filial piety = kin + respect) in multiple categories as necessary. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).

♠ other supernatural enforcement not related to piety ♣ suspected unknown ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_BR]Human Enforcement of Morality
♠ care/harm ♣ present ♥ The Denkard, a compendium of Zoroastrian beliefs dating to the 10th century AD but considered to incorporate much older material, lists a set of virtues which adherents to the faith should cultivate: 'Powerful and resolute endeavor for the sick, the impoverished, and the needy; granting of legal assistance; care and protection and constant eagerness for bringing support to the poor'.[1,119]
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ present ♥ "The individual is encouraged always to choose that which is good in terms of thought, word and action (1.30.2). This choice is encapsulated in the trifold ethic expressed as humata, hukhta, hvareshta - ‘good thoughts, good words, good deeds’ (YH 36.5, 35.2), which remains a seminal manthra for Zoroastrians." [1,120]
♠ in-group loyalty ♣ inferred present ♥ Mary Boyce discusses the strong sense of community among Zoroastrians (she is speaking in a general sense, tracing continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism): 'there is also a striking sense of community, of a bond between all those of "the good religion", united by their common purpose and way of life. This sense of community extends to a remarkable degree to the righteous dead, to those souls "who have overcome for righteousness"'.[1,121]
♠ respect for authority ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ purity ♣ present ♥ "The purity regulations in the central section of the Videvdad reflect the understanding of a material division between ‘good’ and ‘evil’. Discussions about how to dispose of ‘dead matter’ (nasu) so as not to pollute the elements of the world, particularly water, fire or the ashavan, hinge on the distinction between that which brings growth and increase, and that which brings decay. In Videvdad, nasu has become hypostatized as a female demon, [Druj ] Nasush, who pollutes the human body, so that it constantly produces dead matter in the form of effluent such as saliva, mucus and women’s menses. Cuttings of hair and fingernails are also considered as dead matter (Vd 17), and the body of a corpse is the most polluting of all. The ashavan is encouraged always to be on the alert not to let such dead matter near the good creations, and this is why one should not urinate or spit into streams. A female in menses is to isolate herself in a place removed from people, fire and water (Vd 16). Pollution by such dead matter can be removed by cleansing first with bull’s urine and then water (Vd 19.20- 5). The wearing of the kusti is advocated for men and women over 15 years of age as a barrier against the onslaught of evil at both material and conceptual levels (Vd 18.54- 9)." [1,122]
♠ kinship ♣ inferred present ♥ Zoroastrian texts from later periods (e.g. the Middle Persian Arda Wiraz-namag), communicate clearly defined roles and moral duties for worshippers in relation to their family members. '"Righteous women" were those who were acquiescent and conforming, showing "reverence and obedience to their husbands", and abstaining from sin (AWN 13.1-11). Those women who abused or defied their husbands or who ignored their maternal duty to their children would condemn their own souls to hell (AWN 26, 59).'[1,123] Chapter 15 of the Videvdad deals with (among other things) 'a man's obligations to a natural child and its mother'.[1,124] These texts are considered to incorporate older, oral religious traditions.
♠ bravery ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ respect for property ♣ present ♥ Zoroaster's teaching was opposed to the 'taking by force of livestock and other wealth by the strong from the weak and the ravaging of pasturelands, which he saw as disruptions of the natural order'.[1,125]
♠ Moralistic punishment by human agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though information is scant, it seems that crimes were either punished in the family or by the king. "In regard to the administration and organizations of justice in the Parthian period, we are uninformed, except that, in keeping with Parthian tribal tradition, criminal offenses among family members—such as capital crimes committed by brother against sister or father against daughter or among male family members—were settled within the family (Justin, 41.3); they were not subject to judicial process in the law-courts. It is also reported by Philostratus (Life of Apollonius of Tyana 1.25) that the Parthian kings sat in judgement in a specially decorated Babylonian hall in which four revolving golden wheels hung from the ceiling. It was believed that the wheels represented the passage of time, reminding the king that, if he forgot that he is just a human being and thought himself to be more than that, he would rouse the anger of Nemesis (goddess of retributive justice). Although the fabric of the story is based on popular fantasy, reflecting the aspiration for justice rather than a royal, sanctimonious expression of probity, it nevertheless attests the Parthian king’s holding court in special cases, such as, perhaps, capital offenses against the state. However, a certain measure of late Parthian legal practice and judicature may be appraised from the judicial state prevailing under their heirs, the early Sasanians, although PArs, even under the Parthians, had her own specific religious and social environment." [1,126][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Type of punishment this typically entailed (can be more than one):

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy
♠ The state regulates banking ♣ present ♥ "Banking in Iran dates back to 600 BCE ... Private banks, such as those of the Egibi family and the Murashu family, were founded at that time and engaged in mortgages, deposits, and loans. Customers possessed checkbooks and invested in property, farms, slaves, cattle, irrigation projects, fishing, and commercial ships."[1,134] "the Parthians realized the great importance of Greek commercial and banking classes centered at Seleucia"[1,135] "The Babylonian for the most part preserved his civilization, language, and religion unmodified by Greece or Iran. His was the law in western Asia which was least touched by Greek law, and that law still governed internal commerce. While the Greek system of registration of documents was accepted by Babylonians, Greek commercial vocabulary did not supplant that of Babylon."[1,136] Credit and debt present, and enforced legally.[1,137] Regulations for commerce existed but they weren't directly Parthian laws because they were under autonomous states under nominal Parthian authority. However, because they already existed the Parthians didn't need to introduce them.
♠ Price controls ♣ ♥ "the Parthians realized the great importance of Greek commercial and banking classes centered at Seleucia"[1,138] "The Babylonian for the most part preserved his civilization, language, and religion unmodified by Greece or Iran. His was the law in western Asia which was least touched by Greek law, and that law still governed internal commerce. While the Greek system of registration of documents was accepted by Babylonians, Greek commercial vocabulary did not supplant that of Babylon."[1,139] Regulations for commerce existed but they weren't directly Parthian laws because they were under autonomous states under nominal Parthian authority. However, because they already existed the Parthians didn't need to introduce them.
♠ Regulations against hoarding/stockpiling goods ♣ ♥
♠ Regulations regarding usury ♣ inferred present ♥ Credit and debt structures were present and legally enforced.[1,140]
♠ Debt-relief measures ♣ ♥ Credit and debt structures were present and legally enforced.[1,141]
♠ Pensions ♣ ♥
♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ present ♥ Standardized weights and measures had a long history in the region. Standardization of capacity, weight, length, distance in Persia probably goes at least as far back as the Achaemenids (discussion Bivar 2010[1,142]). The standard unit of weight of the shekel "was enforced throughout the Archaemenid Empire by Darius the Great ... around 515 BCE. In this system, the shekel stood at 8.40 grams."[1,143]
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ inferred present ♥ Parthians had coinage and there was a very large mint at Seleucia.[1,144] In the most economically advanced regions (e.g. Mesopotamia, Susiana, Margiana) a "vast quantity of small bronze coins" were minted.[1,145]
♠ Public markets ♣ inferred present ♥ "Parthian cities included palace complexes, fortresses for the military garrison, and temple complexes, as well as markets and residential areas."[1,146][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Contracts

♠ Drinking water ♣ inferred present ♥ knowledge and or infrastructure retained from the Achaemenid Empire era: "Darius ordered the reconstruction of the city of Sarod destroyed by the Greeks. Mendrokles presented to Darius a plan of the city, which was to be built over an area of 50 x 50 Ostad [1 Ostad = 200m]. Piped water and sewers were considered in the plan."[1,150] Fact knowledge may have been retained implied by complex water infrastructure in the new city of Dara: "Tridot or Tirdad, whose foresight rivaled that of Cyrus of Darius built a large city called Dara or Darium or Darius in the year 211 BC near the present day Abivard, to preserve the name of Darius the Great for prosperity. In this city the water flowed in closed conduits and there were provisions for sewers. All houses were equipped with heaters and central heating, which brought steam from a hot water tank to the rooms via a piping system."[1,151]

♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ "Iranians were the inventors of qanats ... during the Archaemenid era there appeared an extensive system of underground networks known as qanats".[1,152] "sites from the Parthian period together with irrigation canals have been found at Qala-i Sam, Kuh-i Khawaja, Sultan Baba Ziyarat"[1,153]

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ inferred present ♥ "Several feasts and festivals are mentioned in textual sources. Spring equinox or the Norouz seems to have been a prominent festival, although not marking the beginning of the year as in later eras. Other harvest related festivals are always mentioned while the Babylonian Astronomical Diaries mention the continuation of old Babylonian and Mesopotamian celebrations, although these might have had only a local acceptance."[1,155]
♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥ "At least in one occasion, a Parthian Emperor is said to have enjoyed plays staged in a Greek style amphitheater, while this might have been an isolated event. We can, however, only speculate about the means of entertainment in the Parthian territories, apart from the religious and cultural festivals in areas such as Babylon."[1,156] "at Nisa a Greek theater was excavated."[1,157] Nisa was a Parthian city.
♠ Theatres ♣ present ♥ "At least in one occasion, a Parthian Emperor is said to have enjoyed plays staged in a Greek style amphitheater, while this might have been an isolated event. We can, however, only speculate about the means of entertainment in the Parthian territories, apart from the religious and cultural festivals in areas such as Babylon."[1,158] "at Nisa a Greek theater was excavated."[1,159] Nisa was a Parthian city.
♠ Arenas ♣ inferred present ♥ Presumably at Greek Seleucia on the Tigris and probably at other Greek cities.
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ e.g temples or the ceremonial halls at Nippur.[1,160] the royal necropolis was at Asaak (Astauene) which was a city established by Arsaces I.[1,161]
♠ Other ♣ ♥
♠ accessibility of entertainment buildings ♣ ♥
♠ Full-time entertainment specialists ♣ present ♥ Musicians. "Many frescoes and even coins depict various musical instruments, including lutes, hand drums, kettle drums, and flutes. Sources such as Greek historians as well as Iranian folk-tales talk about Parthian music and musicians, while scholars have identified various modern local modes to be a combination of local modes and Greek influences."[1,162] "We also know of the presence of wandering singer-musicians in Parthian Armenia who played short-necked lutes which accompanied their singing of epic tales."[1,163] "Greek served as their official language; Greek drama was cultivated at their court ... Parthian poet-musicians (gosans) preserved ancient East Iranian legends, embellishing these with heroic tales from other sources."[1,164]
♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ ♥
♠ Access to education ♣ present ♥ Youth of the elite were trained to be warriors: "there are no reasons to believe that there was any requirement of learning sciences for the noble youth."[1,165] "The philosophical and liberal learning was left largely to the scribal and priestly class who in continuation of the role of similar classes under the Achaemenids, were responsible for the every day running of the government."[1,166] Scribes had "scribal training consisting of memorizing endless administrative terms and their logographic rendering, while their practical training as administrators came from their time apprenticing in the royal or provincial courts."[1,167] They were also likely to have been multi-lingual.[1,168]
♠ Publicly funded or subsidized education ♣ inferred absent ♥ "We cannot be certain of the existence of any organized schooling for lower classes and it is likely that the peasant and artisan classes were illiterate. At the same time, the relative spread of mercantile culture might point out to the existence of some sort of schooling, at least in ancient centers of education and learning like Babylonia."[1,169] Schooling would have been funded privately.
♠ Schools for children? ♣ inferred absent ♥ "We cannot be certain of the existence of any organized schooling for lower classes and it is likely that the peasant and artisan classes were illiterate. At the same time, the relative spread of mercantile culture might point out to the existence of some sort of schooling, at least in ancient centers of education and learning like Babylonia."[1,170] Schooling would have been funded privately.
♠ Universities, “proto-universities”, or other higher education ♣ inferred present ♥ "The Hellenistic commercial aristocracy of the city [Seleucia] supported cultural endeavors, as patrons of literature, art, and academies."[1,171]
♠ Libraries ♣ present ♥ "the Avesta (the holy book of Zoroastrianism) ... credits Vologases I (51-78 CE) with the collection and compiling of the corpus of Avesta itself. Whether true or not, this is an evidence of the interest of later Arsacid Emperors in Zoroastrianism and their possible patronage of its spread."[1,172] "Parthian Empire in particular seems to have become a refuge for the Jews who fled the Roman persecution and brought the fundamental of the rabbinic learning with them, to appear in the form of the Babylonian Talmud a few centuries later."[1,173] "Babylonian astronomy, astrology, mathematics, and medicine were studied and developed by Greek inhabitants of the region [of Babylonia], and Babylonian astrology flooded the western world."[1,174] e.g. at Seleucia.
♠ Museums ♣ ♥
♠ Literacy rate ♣ [5-15] ♥ "We cannot be certain of the existence of any organized schooling for lower classes and it is likely that the peasant and artisan classes were illiterate. At the same time, the relative spread of mercantile culture might point out to the existence of some sort of schooling, at least in ancient centers of education and learning like Babylonia."[1,175] "We know that varied schools of philosophy flourished in Hellenistic Babylonia, and Greek metaphysicians, astronomers, naturalists, historians, geographers, and physicians worked there. The Parthian court made considerable use of such trained and able men for building its bureaucracy."[1,176] "Babylonian astronomy, astrology, mathematics, and medicine were studied and developed by Greek inhabitants of the region [of Babylonia], and Babylonian astrology flooded the western world."[1,177] "Parthian Empire in particular seems to have become a refuge for the Jews who fled the Roman persecution and brought the fundamental of the rabbinic learning with them, to appear in the form of the Babylonian Talmud a few centuries later."[1,178] "The philosophical and liberal learning was left largely to the scribal and priestly class who in continuation of the role of similar classes under the Achaemenids, were responsible for the every day running of the government."[1,179] "the Egyptian philosopher Plotinus made a trip to the Parthian lands alongside the Roman Emperor Caracalla and was much influenced by the intellectual environment of Mesopotamia."[1,180] "The Hellenistic commercial aristocracy of the city [Seleucia] supported cultural endeavors, as patrons of literature, art, and academies."[1,181] At least in Parthian held territories there was probably no schooling funded by state authorities. Learning was for an elite and those who could afford to pay for it, such as wealthy merchants. Literacy in the Parthian Empire would therefore have been confined to the higher classes and the merchant class. Literacy also would have had a geographical cline: much higher in Mesopotamia, among the Babylonians, Greeks, and Jews than in other areas of the Empire. The elite of the Parthians were trained as warriors and apparently 'not required to learn the sciences'.[1,182] When the Parthians sought administrators they often recruited from Hellenistic Babylonia.[1,183]

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ present ♥ The provinces and cities outside the Parthia heartlands paid tribute or allegiance to the ‘king of kings’, but regional lords retained their own power. Example Characene? regained c150/151 CE: "With the amount of trade going through that land, its independence was a loss of much revenue to the Parthians."[1,187] "The provincial government was divided into two systems. The first was the system of the client kingdoms and the second, provinces or satrapis ran much like the Achaemenid model. The client kingdoms of the Arsacid Empire, mainly clustered in the western and southern portions of the empire, were ruled by local dynasties and were considered personal clients of the Arsacid king. These had a high degree of internal autonomy and administrative freedom, often being only responsible for paying an annual tribute to the court."[1,188] "At the beginning of the Arsacid rule, kingdoms of Osrhoene, Gordiane, Adiabene in northern Mesopotamia/Assyria, Characene in Babylon, Elymais in Elam, and that of the Frataraka in Persis were identifiable. However, these soon succumbed to the increasing Arsacid power and were reduced to client kingdoms and vassals, preserving their local dynasties as vassals of the Arsacids and subjected to annual tributes."[1,189]
♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ Ostraca texts: "The ostraca suggest that the Parthian kings could rely on revenues in kind from their own estates."[1,190] Taxes could be paid in kind, such as in wine.[1,191]
♠ Labor ♣ ♥
♠ Currency ♣ inferred present ♥ "Mining may also have been an important source of state revenue".[1,192] Also various forms of trade revenue would have brought in currency. "The Arsacids depended in large measure upon their own land holdings and on direct commercial duties, rather than on taxes of agricultural produce imposed by a central regime uniformly on all territories."[1,193][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Debt and Indemnities
♠ Polity has obligation to other polity or group ♣ ♥

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥ "Taxes do not appear to have weighed heavily on the subjects. The Arsacids depended in large measure upon their own land holdings and on direct commercial duties, rather than on taxes of agricultural produce imposed by a central regime uniformly on all territories."[1,194] "the decrees and treaties with Hellenistic city-states where an effort was often made by the local population to negotiate and fix the amount of tax."[1,195] The state fixed and strictly controlled tax revenue."[1,196] "Records found in excavations at Nisa provide evidence of different types of tax collection, depending on the category of the land. Two categories are known - patbaz and uzbari. Patbaz was collection in kind for the use of the king. It is less clear what the other category was. There are also indications of the existence of special levies for the support of religious activities, somewhat similar to tithes."[1,197] Mithradates recovered territory in Central Asia and eastern Iran which "opened land routes between China and the Mediterranean world. Since they all crossed Arsacid imperial territories, revenues from duties and taxes on luxury goods being transported became an important source of wealth for Parthian rulers.".[1,198] "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to chose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[1,199] "Records found in excavations at Nisa provide evidence of different types of tax collection ... There are also indications of the existence of special levies for the support of religious activities, somewhat similar to tithes."[1,200][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Taxes are imposed on:
♠ Land ♣ present ♥ "The basic tax was a land-tax, imposed on the farmers in form of a rent, payable to the local large or petty land-owners who collected and dispatched the tax to the royal court or the local administration. This tax was almost exclusively extracted in kind and was often redistributed locally as well as being used for provisioning the garrisons."[1,201] Excavations at Nisa provide evidence for land taxation: patbaz "collection in kind for the use of the king" and uzbari, the nature of which is unknown.[1,202]
♠ Property ♣ ♥
♠ People ♣ inferred present ♥ head-taxes "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to chose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[1,203] "In cities, royal decrees demanded a head-tax extracted from all male, working people and from the heads of families; children, women, and the elderly being exempt."[1,204]
♠ Trade ♣ present ♥ There was diplomatic contact with the Chinese. A Parthian general met a Chinese envoy in c.199 BCE.[1,205] "The conditions Strabo describes on the Euphrates frontier (XVI.1.27) may have prevailed elsewhere in the Parthian Empire: local strongmen collected tolls to whatever level the traffic would bear and passed as little as possible on to the royal treasury. Various types of land taxes and head tax also constituted the great bulk of all the tax revenues collected in the Sasanian Empire."[1,206] Mithradates II c100 BCE recovered territory in Central Asia and eastern Iran which "opened land routes between China and the Mediterranean world. Since they all crossed Arsacid imperial territories, revenues from duties and taxes on luxury goods being transported became an important source of wealth for Parthian rulers."[1,207] "Roads were dotted with resting places or Caravanserais ... usually maintained by the local rulers, which provided the merchants and messengers, as well as their animals, an easy access to food, water, and fodder."[1,208] "Parthian merchants made huge profits by reselling Chinese wares, mainly silk, on the markets of the Roman Empire. ... This intermediary trade enriched the Parthian merchant class, and the state obtained a substantial revenue from trading dues. Archaeological finds from Seleucia on the Tigris and written records point to the existence of taxes on trade in slaves, salt and other items."[1,209]
♠ Income ♣ ♥
♠ Production ♣ inferred present ♥ other levies from their wage labor "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to chose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[1,210][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Taxes are imposed by:
♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ present ♥ "Taxes do not appear to have weighed heavily on the subjects. The Arsacids depended in large measure upon their own land holdings and on direct commercial duties, rather than on taxes of agricultural produce imposed by a central regime uniformly on all territories."[1,211] Direct commerical duties may have been imposed and collected by state officials.
♠ local elites ♣ present ♥ "Local chiefs were also responsible for the collection of taxes, which were often paid in kind, and distribution of supplies."[1,212] "The governor was responsible for the administration of the satrapi as well as the collection of taxes, and he was also the ultimate military ruler of the province."[1,213]
♠ state officials ♣ inferred present ♥ "Taxes do not appear to have weighed heavily on the subjects. The Arsacids depended in large measure upon their own land holdings and on direct commercial duties, rather than on taxes of agricultural produce imposed by a central regime uniformly on all territories."[1,214] Direct commerical duties may have been imposed and collected by state officials.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Type of taxes paid:
♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ "Local chiefs were also responsible for the collection of taxes, which were often paid in kind, and distribution of supplies."[1,215] "Records found in excavations at Nisa provide evidence of different types of tax collection, depending on the category of the land. Two categories are known - patbaz and uzbari. Patbaz was collection in kind for the use of the king. It is less clear what the other category was. There are also indications of the existence of special levies for the support of religious activities, somewhat similar to tithes."[1,216] Taxes could be paid in kind, such as in wine.[1,217] "Parthian merchants made huge profits by reselling Chinese wares, mainly silk, on the markets of the Roman Empire. ... This intermediary trade enriched the Parthian merchant class, and the state obtained a substantial revenue from trading dues. Archaeological finds from Seleucia on the Tigris and written records point to the existence of taxes on trade in slaves, salt and other items."[1,218]
♠ Labor ♣ ♥ No mention of corvee labour or a labour tax in sources consulted.
♠ Currency ♣ present ♥ "From the large number of coins minted by both the Arsacid state and the client kingdoms, we can guess that monetary taxation occupied a position of prominence and might have formed a large part of urban tax extraction."[1,219]

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ ♥
♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ ♥
♠ Top fortune ♣ ♥
♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ ♥ ♠ Dedicated police force ♣ inferred present ♥ Alexander the Great c330 BCE with the Macedonian conquest of Persia introduced the Greek Polis system which organized the law and order in the cities. "It is very likely that the term police in Western languages has its source in the Greek notion of Polis.” Under this system "An official named Epistat, appointed by the monarchy, oversaw the manner in which the Polis performed its duties.”[1,220]
♠ Police levels ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Proscriptions of elites and or property ♣ ♥ Use of the state to repress or purge rival elite groups.
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ ♥
♠ Military control over independent or semi-independent vassal states ♣ present ♥ The Parthian Empire was divided into three areas: self governing kingdoms in Mesopotamia; semi-independent kingdoms on the borders of Iran); and Parthian 'heartlands' in the centre under rule of the King.[1,221] "Written documents (mainly in Greek, Latin, or Hebrew) from the first two centuries A.D. in Southwest Asia suggest that the Parthian 'Empire' was at most times an unstable coalition of vassal states brought periodically under imperial Parthian control."[1,222] "until AD 224, when the Parthian empire was overthrown by one of its vassal kings, the Sassanid Persian Ardashir.”[1,223]
